Preface
This thesis is written in relation to the master thesis 'Enabling oshore structural integrity
monitoring by utilizing underwater vehicles' by Toke Malm, master student at DTU Design
and Innovation, and Christian Espersen, master student at DTU Mechanical Engineering.
The project was supervised by Niels Henrik Mortensen (DTU Mechanical Engineering)
and David Johan Christensen (DTU Electrical Engineering).
Toke Malm
Christian Jacob Friis Espersen
Kongens Lyngby, 7 March 2016

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The oshore oil and gas industry in the North Sea faces an imminent problem of ag-
ing platforms. New advanced production technologies grants access to new challenging
reservoirs, but the design-specied life expectancy of these platforms is increasingly being
exceeded. The key to extending the life of the platforms is to employ rigorous inspection
strategies and advanced monitoring technologies. Inspection has traditionally been costly,
mainly due to the man-hours primarily invested in manual processes and the complexity
of deploying these processes.
The emerging technology of autonomous inspection vehicles has the potential to reduce
overall logistics and deployment complexity. However monitoring technologies are limited
by particular fundamental obstacles such as battery life and the diculty of transmitting
data through water. The implementation of a monitoring system, utilizing autonomous
inspection vehicles, has the potential to mitigate the limitations of both manual inspections
and monitoring tasks.
This thesis presents a novel integrated inspection and monitoring system, which enables
greater condence in structural reliability of oshore oil and gas platforms. A feasibility
study is presented of the potential capabilities and practical limitations of autonomous
underwater robots in oshore applications . This leads to a holistic system design, where
the key component is the monitoring unit. The monitoring unit allows operators to monitor
the health of their platforms. Subsea structure movement and strain measurements were
identied as the most applicable monitoring technologies in terms of usability and maturity.
The mounting of the monitoring unit was identied as a key function. An experimental
setup was devised in order to prove the concept of adhesively mounting the monitoring
unit underwater onto a base equivalent of an typical oil platform structure.
The test
indicated that a high strength bond is achievable even if the adhesive is applied and cured
underwater.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The oil and gas platforms in the Danish North Sea region are aging and production volume
is declining. The current oil prices put pressure on prot margins of oil and gas production.
In addition more demanding safety regulations were added after the Deepwater Horizon
disaster in 2010, this has lead to increased requirements for documentation. The estab-
lished oil and gas elds in the Danish North Sea are the easiest exploitable elds but
they will be depleted within the next few decades (Andersen, 2014). Already within ve
years Denmark will no longer be self-sucient in fossil fuels and will become dependent
on foreign exporters, even though large quantities of oil and gas still remain in the Danish
underground. New explored oil and gas elds are more remote, on deeper water and have
a more challenging reservoir characteristics. Subsea wells has been established on some of
these new elds. The wells are connected to existing production platforms. However the
elds require additional production and processing equipment. This equipment can only
be placed on existing platforms, changing their orginally intended use.
These issues of aging platforms, increasing safety requirements and change platform use,
creates a demand for new technology to ensure the safe extension of service life for existing
platforms. In this light an increase in platform inspections and damage history documen-
tation are a necessity. Due to the promise of cost-eectiveness and exibility, the oil and
gas industry has seen great potential in autonomous inspection and monitoring systems.
Within the industry, autonomous underwater vehicles has found a niche application in
seabed and pipeline survey. However the drag optimized prole for these applications does
not translate well into the complex and deft movements required for structural inspection.
However less than a decade ago the rst autonomous vehicle capable of visual inspection
and simple intervention work was developed (Mair et al., 2010). This hover-capable au-
tonomous vehicle has only recently been ocean tested in July 2014 (Oshore Energy Today,
2014).
Autonomous underwater robots have been an academic interest for decades. The Technical
University of Denmark (DTU) has recently launched a new underwater robotics develop-
==PAGE:18==
2
Introduction
ment platform, called REMORA1 (Christensen et al., 2014). The objective of this project
is to develop a platform for cost-eective and modular autonomous robots. The REMORA
project was the starting point for this thesis and our objective is to rene and detail the
practical applications for the REMORA project. The feasibility of autonomous robots is
explored, and we aim to propose a complete system that is both technically viable and can
provide sucient detailing to enable a real-world application and implementation.
This thesis will focus on designing a system for oshore installations that utilizes the
emerging technologies in hover-capable autonomous underwater vehicles. The scope of
this thesis is restricted geographically to the Danish region of the North Sea2. We explore
the inspection and monitoring of oshore steel structures, in particular the jackup and xed
platforms in the oshore oil and gas sector. Although oshore wind farms are experiencing
similar aging and structural issues, we have chosen to exclude this industry. The reason for
this decision is our assessment that the necessity for autonomous inspection is greater in
the oil and gas industry, mainly due to the age of the structures compared to the relatively
young age of current oshore wind farms. Furthermore the safety requirements are higher
in the oil and gas industry due to the high human and environmental consequences of
failure.
The approach in this thesis has been to survey the market for oshore inspection trends
and the overall industry characteristics that inevitably will inuence the design of the
system. We aim to identify the critical areas in inspection and monitoring, that ultimately
will create the most cost-eective system. These areas are then evaluated in terms of the
ability to be automated, using autonomous vehicles.
The end goal is twofold, rstly we wish to design a holistic system concept, which include
a high degree of autonomy in the context of real-world logistics and managerial challenges.
Secondly to create a detailed concept of a monitoring unit, where the main functions of the
unit are identied and linked directly to the interface design of the monitoring unit. The
scope of this thesis is not to present a nished product for production, but a concept based
on a mechanically feasible design. Lastly we provide a list of areas where further work
could be very benecial for the continued development of the product design presented in
this thesis.
The structure of this thesis are presented in the following. In the next chapter 2 Methodology,
the chosen method of designing a system is presented. In chapter 3 Current state of the
oshore industry, the overall state of the oshore oil and gas industry is discussed, and the
market opportunities in inspection and monitoring are identied. Chapter 4 Inspection
and monitoring of subsea oshore structures, discusses the technology used for inspections
and monitoring, and how far the industry is in enabling autonomous underwater inspec-
tions. In chapter 5 Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system) the
overall system is developed and presented, and in chapter 6 Monitoring concept, a detailed
concept of our monitoring unit is developed. Lastly, chapter 7 Function proof-of-concept
testing, describes the process of testing some of the key functions of the monitoring unit.
An acronym list and a glossary of the relevant industry terms can be found in the back
matter respectively on page 151 and on page 153.
1REcongurable MOdular Robotic system for Aquatic environments
2Information of this particular geographical region is more widely available through the Danish Hy-
drocarbon Center for Research and Technology (DHCRT)

1.1 Project plan
3
1.1
Project plan
This project ran its course of six months from September 2015 to March 2016. The original
structure of the project was divided into six phases each lasting roughly one month. These
phases are listed below along with the goals for each phase. The rst phase consists of a
literature review where the characteristics of the industry were studied. Initially none of
us had any experience with the oshore industry. Thus an important part of the project
were to gain a comprehensive understanding of the oshore industry, the current processes
of inspecting subsea structures and the opportunities of implementing autonomous robots.
1. Literature study and industry interviews (1 month)
(a) Scope the project
(b) Identify the market and technological opportunities
(c) Identify the requirements
2. System concept generation (1 month)
(a) Select a promising system concept
3. Monitoring unit concept generation (2 weeks)
(a) Select a promising monitoring unit concept
4. Monitoring unit detail design (1 month)
(a) Produce a prototype
5. Testing of monitoring unit (2 weeks)
(a) Test a key function
6. Writing of the report (1 month)
(a) Deliver report
However about a month into the project, the plan was revised as the time allotted for
the research was insucient, as shown below. We realized that we needed more time to
identify opportunities and understand the requirements of the industry.
1. Literature study and industry interviews (3 months)
(a) Scope the project
(b) Identify the market and technological opportunities
(c) Identify the requirements
2. System concept generation (2 weeks)
(a) Select a promising system concept
3. Monitoring unit concept generation (2 weeks)
(a) Select a promising monitoring unit concept
4. Monitoring unit detail design (3 weeks)
(a) Produce a prototype
5. Testing of monitoring unit (1 week)
(a) Test key function(s) and document the results
6. Writing of the report (1 month)
(a) Deliver report

Chapter 2
Methodology
This chapter will provide a discussion on the choice of forming an interdisciplinary team,
and the engineering approach chosen in this research, in order to solve the problem state-
ment.
Interdisciplinary team
According to (Halbe, Adamowski, and Pahl-Wostl, 2015) engineering tasks have seen a
transition, shifting its focus from technical issues towards a more holistic approach to
problem solving, requiring an integrated, adaptive and participatory approach. Although
the common engineering method is usually associated with a predict and control paradigm
based on multidisciplinary an emerging expectation is the interdisciplinary approach. The
(Halbe et al., 2013). The levels of disciplinarity is shown in g. 2.1.
In this research it has been an active decision to combine our two dierent, although
overlapping disciplines; mechanical engineering and design engineering. The purpose of
enabling an interdisciplinary team to solve a problem reaching beyond the boundaries
of the individual disciplines. The ambition is to complement dierent methods with a
Figure 2.1: Overview of dierent levels of disciplinarity (Jensenius, 2012)
==PAGE:22==
6
Methodology
nuanced view. This is realized by the statement that problems of the real world are not
organized according to academic disciplines (Muzafer Shenf, 1969). However we're limited
by our team size, and in order to cover the full set of disciplines needed, we would have
required competencies in electrical, chemical, structural and signal processing engineering.
Design approach
The initiative taken in this project was a constructivist approach that continued on the
scope of the REMORA project (Christensen et al., 2014). The design approach chosen was
a classic development approach. The multi-faced task of improving oshore inspection was
studied and we set out to identify the competitive and technological opportunities that
could improve processes and solve the problems.
The focus was to facilitate the process of designing an innovative and holistic product, by
incorporating known literature, emerging technology and considering the industry as a key
stakeholder.
Through the process of critical reection of assumptions and questioning current trends,
the transformative learning experience created a diverse view on the inspection industry
and inuenced the preconceived notion on utilizing autonomous robots and the complexity
of inspection. (Mezirow, 1997). This lead us to view the problem from dierent angles
and change our approach from the rst initial direction.
The chosen research approach were based on an empirical and interpretive method. The
research was carried out early on with qualitative interviews and a literature study. This
identied the environment of the oshore industry and the work ow of subsea inspection.
The industry was characterized using the model Porter's Five Forces (Jones et al., 2010).
The Power Tower model (Mortensen et al., 2012) assisted in pin pointing the market
opportunity space. Topics such as product complexity assisted in making conscious de-
sign choices when adding complexity and platform design philosophy guided our concept
selection, based on the belief that one size ts no-one.
Development and design were driven by a systematic design approach for industrial prod-
ucts, guided by the Eight stage model, see g. 2.2 on the next page. In this model the
iterative process through the classic engineering phases enables a successful product de-
velopment.
Developing and mapping the design objectives and requirements are done using diverse
methods such as objective tree and function-means analysis. These objectives and require-
ments have been continuously questioned through interviews with industry and have been
related to scientic papers. This iterative process has lead to a greater understanding
of the challenges and identied many of the inuencing requirements of the oshore in-
spection industry. A categorized landscape of these requirements further contributed to
our existing body of knowledge and enabled an analysis of the challenges of implementing
autonomous systems.
After the design solution space had been bounded by the set of requirements, a broad range
of alternative concepts were generated using means morphology. When generating these
==PAGE:23==
7
Figure 2.2: Eight stages of the design process within the symmetrical problem/solution
model (Cross, 2008)
alternative concepts a systematic approach to evaluate the best performing features of each
concept were then sought to be incorporated in the next concept generation iteration. The
goal was to design a concept which is superior in the most important characteristics. All
the alternative concepts were then rated and selected using a decision matrix (Ullman,
2003).

Chapter 3
Current state of the oshore
industry
This chapter will briey explore the oil and gas industry's current state and investigate the
many challenges the industry faces. The trends in the inspection industry are described
and the market opportunities identied and summarized.
3.1
The economic pressure
The oil and gas industry provides the world with energy and the oil and gas platform
operator's main focus is ensuring a stable production. As of early 2016 the oil prices have
plunged by over 70 % below 30$ per barrel, the steepest downfall since the 1990's. Company
earnings have declined and accordingly two-thirds of the rigs have been decommissioned
due to investments cuts in exploration and production (Times, 2016).
Figure 3.1: Historic oil prices from the last decade in USD (Times, 2016). Prices are
lower now than at the nancial crisis in the late 2008.
==PAGE:26==
10
Current state of the oshore industry
The pressure on earnings naturally puts pressure on cutting operational costs, extending
the operation of existing platforms and delaying investments in developing new elds.
These new elds are characterized by being in much deeper water and further from shore,
thus requiring higher investments. Some of these elds are designated as High Pressure
High Temperature (HPHT) elds, which require new reservoir and production technology.
The global trends can be seen in g. 3.2 on the facing page. The gure clearly shows a
rising share of oshore deepwater and that the expanded production capacity in production
has almost been entirely in the oshore deepwater region.
The danish consumption of oil has been stable, or in slight recession, over the last 30 years
and are projected to continue that trend, see g. 3.3 on the next page. However production
is projected to fall in the near short term, and the breaking point where Denmark is no
longer self sucient in oil is only ve years away.
In g. 3.3 on the facing page the
estimated Danish production of oil is shown with its peak around 2004.
The graph's
production prole is divided into four dierent regions. There is the historical production
region and the expected production region. Then the region called technological resources,
which is the reserves, from existing elds, that is expected to be retrievable with new
production technology, such as HPHT . Lastly there are the prospective resources, which
are the resources from discovered elds that have not been developed yet.
In summary the production in existing elds is expected to be stable in the short term (to
around 2021) and then fall in the longer term (Andersen, 2014). However it is expected
that existing elds will produce for the next 10 to 20 years, but will be more demanding
in multiple aspects of production and safety.

3.1 The economic pressure

Current state of the oshore industry
3.2
Subsea technology push and pull
The current easy accessible elds are facing depletion and the industry is pushed to deeper,
colder, more remote elds. These require more demanding production equipment to exploit
the increasingly challenging reservoirs. For this reason the industry is adding more func-
tionalities to already existing production equipment (Petroleumtilsynet, 2014). As the cost
increase with the complexity of the wells, the operators has to develop cost-ecient ways
to produce, process and transport uids. An emerging trend is to install more processing
equipment, termed XTs, remotely on the sea oor1.
As the wells near the processing platforms deplete, new and more remote wells are installed.
However as these move further away from the processing platforms, the complexity of
linking these wells back to the old processing platforms increase. An entwined web of
pipelines is created when new remote wells are connected back to old platforms. This
system is called a tie-back system and in most cases a tie-back solution necessitate more
production equipment on the platform. The processing platforms has not been designed
for the additional weight of new production equipment and therefore increasing the load
on the structure.
3.3
Safety regulation
The safety of oil and gas production is of very high priority, as the Deepwater Horizon
disaster in the Gulf of Mexico in 2010 highlights. Large scale accidents can have huge
environmental impact. The oil and gas industry is a major environmental and safety risk
for society and therefore failure is not an option. The incentives of the industry is to
maximise production while minimizing risks as low as reasonably possible. However the
industry is regulated by various industry standards from Norwegian petroleum industry
(Norsok, 2004) and American Petroleum Institute (API, 2002) and dierent governmental
agencies such as the European Commission. All regulators set out regulations that require
operators to document a minimum level of safety and to implement risk reducing measures.
Three of the seven EU safety standards2 states: (European Commission, 2016)
• national authorities must verify safety provisions, environmental protection mea-
sures, and the emergency preparedness of rigs and platforms. If companies do not
respect the minimum standards, EU countries can impose sanctions, including halt-
ing production
• information on how companies and EU countries keep installations safe must be
made available for citizens
• companies will be fully liable for environmental damages caused to protected marine
species and natural habitats. For damage to marine habitats, the geographical zone
1processing equipment such as wellheads, multifaced pumpstations, separation units, hydraulics, man-
ifolds etc.
2Under the Safety of Oshore Oil and Gas Operations Directive, the EU has put in place a set of rules
to help prevent accidents, as well as respond promptly and eciency should one occur.
==PAGE:29==
3.4 The aging platforms in the Danish North Sea
13
will cover all EU marine waters including exclusive economic zones and continental
shelves.
3.4
The aging platforms in the Danish North Sea
The Danish part of the North Sea consists of about 400 wells distributed on 19 oil and gas
elds with about 55 oshore platforms, like the one shown in g. 3.4. The largest operator
is Maersk Oil & Gas with 15 elds, DONG is operator on three elds and Hess on one
eld. See appendix C on page 241 for a map of the Danish North Sea.
The main part of the elds in the Danish North Sea were developed in the period between
the 1980's and the 1990's. Many of these platforms are either approaching or exceeded their
original intended life. This presents new challenges for the structural integrity management
of these platforms. Consequently, a lot of mature oshore platforms are going through
rehabilitation, designed to increase or maintain their structural reliability, and with some
even being introduced to more demanding operation than they were previously designed
for (see appendix A.8 on page 188).
The age of the oshore platforms can be seen in g. 3.5 on page 15.
A similar trend
can be observed for the Norwegian and UK region, see (Galbraith and Sharp, 2007; May
et al., 2009). The design life of oshore platforms in the North Sea are generally about
25 years (Galbraith and Sharp, 2007; Stacey, Birkinshaw, and Sharp, 2008). There is a
persistent requirement that the platforms continue operation, either for production of oil
and gas in their current eld or to serve as a base for adjacent subsea installations. Thus
the structures are likely to continue to be in operation for a signicant time beyond their
design life.
The distribution of platform age in the Danish elds shows that currently half of the plat-
forms has exceeded their original intended life. If we assume that the platforms will be
decommissioned when the reserves deplete then an estimation of their age at decommis-
sioning can be made. A rough calculation of the expected age of structures at end-of-life
Figure 3.4: Maersk GIANT oil platform in the North Sea (Andy Buchanan, 2016)
==PAGE:30==
14
Current state of the oshore industry
are shown in g. 3.6 on the next page. The estimate was calculated using the estimated oil
reserves in the eld compared with the most recent yearly production volume (Andersen,
2014). The conclusion is that we expect around two thirds of the total amount of oshore
platforms will require some degree of life extension as of now.

3.5
Life extension for aging platforms
Oshore platforms are designed to last a nite amount of time, called the design life,
according to standards based on limit states, usually ultimate, serviceability, fatigue and
accident damage, see (Norsok, 2004; API, 2002). In the case of life extension the service
life of a platform is extended beyond the design life. This leads to the number of loading
cycles, that the structure experiences, are also increased. Thus life extension is directly
aecting the fatigue limit state, however, cracking from other causes can also aect the
reserve strength and in this way also the ultimate limit state for the platform.
The structural integrity of platforms is ensured based on a combination of inspections and
modelling. In both cases approximations and assumptions has to be made, for eaxmple in
terms of the magnitude and the frequency of extreme loading scenarios. Two approaches
can be employed to boost condence in face of uncertainty. The rst is the implementation
of conservative safety factors. This approach results in over-designed structures which are
consequently heavier and more environmentally and nancially costly to operate.
The alternative is to use marginal safety factors, which is especially true for weight crit-
ical structures. The requirements of this approach involves monitoring of environmental
conditions, and that any damage defect due to unexpected loading is swiftly detected,
located and categorized. The signicance of the defect in terms of structural performance
is assessed and appropriate mitigation techniques can be implemented.
Safety factors for the fatigue limit state recommended by standards vary from two for a
conventional welded joint inspectable in service, to ten for a critical non-inspectable joint,
see g. 3.7 on the facing page (Galbraith and Sharp, 2007). However early designs rarely
incorporated the larger safety factors and thus do not meet current standard requirements.
This makes old platforms more prone to fatigue eects.
Aging structures in the North Sea are characterized by deterioration which is mainly caused
by corrosion and fatigue. Where fatigue usually is the governing failure mode due to the
harsh environment of the North Sea. The aging process aects the structural integrity of
the structure and over time the accumulated damage increases as illustrated on g. 3.8
on the next page. The rate of deterioration is shown in g. 3.9 on page 18. As noted the
damage rate is constant through most of the service life, however as the structure reaches
the end of its designed life the risk of failure increase sharply with time, unless managed.
Life extension involves an assessment of the structures performance in terms of fatigue
and corrosion. This assessment is especially important for platforms where conditions has
changed since the design, e.g. the use of the platform has changed or due to climate change.
However there is considerable uncertainty in the assessment process, mainly due to incom-
plete historic fatigue information and inherent physical randomness in the deterioration
processes. The assessment requirements of life extension has only recently been formally
recognized by the industry. Mainly due to recent new regulatory requirements for oshore
structures. A list of relevant regulatory codes can be found in (Stacey, Birkinshaw, and
Sharp, 2008).
An important requirement in the structural integrity assessment of aging structures and

life extension is detailed information from inspections, both at the fabrication stage and

3.6 Oshore structural integrity and reliability management
19
3.6
Oshore structural integrity and reliability management
Structural Integrity (SI) has been described as the ability of a structure to retain its
strength, function and shape within acceptable limits, without failure when subjected to
the loads imposed throughout the structures's service life by operation within the limita-
tions of the designed use (Structural Integrity Handbook Issue 1.1 ).
In order to assess the problems in providing satisfactory life extension as described in the
previous section, there has been a great interest from regulatory side to implement more
comprehensive and robust structural health monitoring systemsSHM (May et al., 2009;
Wintle and Sharp, 2008; Galbraith and Sharp, 2007). A structural health monitoring sys-
tem provides continuous data about the performance of a structure, and has the potential
to reduce inspection costs while providing greater condence in structural integrity and
thus in safety.
There is an ever growing trend to explore the technologies and methods to extend the
lifespan of oshore structures, but there is no actual regulatory requirements for the use
of specic SI monitoring and methods in order to extend the lifespan.
3.6.1
Trends in structural integrity monitoring
The benecial factors of implementing a Structural Integrity Monitoring (SIM) system
can, in combination with inspection methods, be used to assess the risk of an oshore
structure that has exceeded its original design life. The advantages can be listed as follows
(May et al., 2009; Petroleumtilsynet, 2014)
• Monitoring of a known local defect or high risk part of a structure
• Lowering structural integrity risks
• Justication of a reduction in inspection requirements
• Reduction in skilled oshore workers
• Cost reduction for new build structures
• Reduction of safety factors in mechanical design
• Satisfying regulator requirements
The Danish Hydrocarbon Research and Technology Center has identied three interesting
research areas (see appendix A.11 on page 198), in the Response Monitoring category, as
high priority.
• Condition based structural model calibration with optimal sensors in terms of type
and location.
• Real time fatigue life monitoring with low cost instrumentation by utilizing Opera-
tional Modal Analysis.
• Technology scout for sensors measuring subsea acceleration and strain
==PAGE:36==
20
Current state of the oshore industry
In regards to the scope of this project the most interesting trend is the move towards
monitoring assisted modeling, where actual values are measured and incorporated into
the models. However current systems have to a large extent only been deployed topside.
Introducing monitoring systems below the water line increases complications mainly due to
the costs and safety hazards associated with divers or the operation cost of large workclass
remote operated vehicles (ROV). The experience with ROV deployed SIM sensors are
therefore limited and few oer these solutions, see power tower, see g. 3.11 on page 22.
An example of a monitoring system is the DeepData Pod from Fugro in which sensors are
structurally mounted using magnets. The system uses accelerometers and angular rate
sensors with up to 6 degrees of freedom. The system is shown in g. 3.10.
Figure 3.10: The commercially available monitoring unit from Fugro. The unit contains
an accelerometer and hydroacoustic modem and is used for riser or pipeline
monitoring (Fugro DeepData 2015).
3.6.2
Trends in inspection
As stated in previous sections, there have been an increase in subsea installations and their
complexity along with a growing number of aging platforms in the recent years. All of
which increases the demand and frequency for subsea inspections. A major safety concern
is the use of divers for inspection of production sites on deep water. Although the trend is
moving towards more ROV operations, the technology are not fully capable of replacing
the precision an inspection diver oers.
Our research has identied the following desired improvements for inspection, see (Straub
et al., 2006; May et al., 2009; McLeod, 2010), interview in appendix A.8 on page 188 and
interview in appendix A.6 on page 178.
• Reduction in use of divers
• More frequent inspection
• Less cumbersome inspections
==PAGE:37==
3.7 Inspection market competition
21
• Better structure monitoring
• Reducing support ship requirement
• Faster mobilization and demobilization of required infrastructure
• Reduction in oshore crew and skills requirements
• No risk of platform damage
Currently even minor Inspection, Maintenance and Repair (IMR) operations such as visual
inspection and light manipulation work require dedicated inspection vessels to deploy divers
or large ROVs. These technologies are often under-utilized when performing these tasks
and bear a high cost in terms of crew, maintenance and logistics (McLeod, 2010).
The industry is pursuing two paths, one that focuses on developing more sophisticated
inspection technologies, thus increasing the probability of detection for damage.
This
path faces many technological challenges. The other path involves increasing the inspec-
tion frequency and thereby increasing the probability of detection and faces logistical and
managerial challenges.
Currently the limiting factor is the high cost of conducting an inspection mission.
It
is simply not feasible to increase inspections compared to the value of the information.
However if a system is relying on simpler technology that is cheap to deploy then more
frequent inspections can be enabled. Such a system might prove to be very competitive
and cost-eective within the current industry.
A new development is the introduction
of the hover-capable Underwater Autonomous Vehicle (AUV). These vehicles allow the
automation of simple inspection tasks and may disrupt the current Inspection, Maintenance
and Repair (IMR) industry when more capabilities are developed. AUVs are discussed in
more detail in section 4.5.3 on page 58.
3.7
Inspection market competition
The subsea inspection industry is a billion dollar industry and is dominated by a few
large corporations which provide services such as construction, installation, inspection,
maintenance, repair and decommissioning of oshore installations.
These corporations
have tailored their services to provide a one-stop-shop for their customers through smaller
contractors.
When we mapped the competition, a range of these companies appears,
covering from small rms for specialized inspection tasks to larger companies managing
inspection operations.
To compete with the established inspection companies, a signicant value proposition
must be presented in order to penetrate the industry. Ideally a new system that adds
signicant value to the operator on current operations and creates a demand in the industry.
Introducing a system based on new technology would be a challenge due to the very
conservative industry (Bonica, 2014). The industry is very risk-adverse and only minor
incremental real-world tests occur. New technology is slowly adopted based primarily on
the track record of the technology, see appendix A.1 on page 167. Implementing products
for radical improvements may therefore be a challenge.

22
Current state of the oshore industry
Within the market for IMR services, tasks can be divided into three categories. Visual
inspection, Non-destructive Testing (NDT) inspection and monitoring.

BARRIER TO ENTRY
The industry is characterized by being very conservative and risk-adverse. New players

professional divers while remotely operated vehicles are mostly deployed when the task

3.8
Market opportunities
On the basis of the preliminary research it shows that the oil and gas industry are facing
great challenges in the subsea inspection. Trends are showing new market opportunities
opened by new technology, new regulations and new demands, that have not been covered
yet.
The biggest trend we are seeing is an increasing requirement for more inspections and
a demand for more extensive structural integrity monitoring, in order to comply with
safety- and environmental regulations, optimizing production and preventing catastrophic
disasters. There is a general push in the industry towards increasing data collection and
monitoring of structures. The goal is to improve safety and production while reducing cost
and time.
Autonomous inspection vehicles has considerable disruptive potential driven by cost-eective
inspections and reduced human risks. However there is even greater opportunities in com-
bining structural integrity monitoring and simple inspections while minimizing the reliance
on platform systems.

• Why are structures damaged and why are subsea inspections required?

4.1
The North Sea
The North Sea is a large semi-enclosed sea in the north-west of Europe. The North Sea
is bordered by the countries of Norway, Sweden, Denmark, Germany, The Netherlands,
Belgium, France, England and Scotland. The sea is delimited by the western approach to
the English Channel (5 ◦W), the northern Atlantic between Scotland and Norway (62 ◦N,
5 ◦W) and the Baltic in the Danish Straits. The North Sea is primarily a shallow sea
==PAGE:42==
26
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
with an average depth of 95 meters, though it is getting deeper towards the Norwegian
Trench with depths up to 725 meters. The North Sea is one of the world's most important
shing grounds. The main inow of water is from the Atlantic in the north. Surface water
temperature varies between 0 to 20 ◦C, depending on the season and the part of the sea,
with less variation in the north. The salinity of the open North Sea varies between 32 to
34.5 psu. In the coastal areas of Skagerrak salinity ranges between 25 to 34 psu and in the
Wadden Sea it is usually less than 30 psu. Temperature and salinity show variability at
annual, seasonal and decadal scales. Tidal currents vary from some of the strongest in the
world to zero. Decreased oxygen levels are found below 70 meter (Walday and Kroglund,
2011). Salinity, temperature and oxygen levels are all important factors in the corrosion
process of steel.
The concentration of dissolved materials in the sea varies greatly with location and time as
seawater is diluted by melting ice, rivers, rain or concentrated by evaporation. Generally
ocean water has high concentration of salts, mainly sodium chloride. Gases are soluble
in sea water, where oxygen and carbon dioxide are of particular interest in regards to
corrosion. Sea water has relatively high electrical conductivity (around 4 S/m) and high
pH value. Biological life are present which can be grouped in micro-fouling, e.g. bacteria
and slime and macro-fouling, e.g.
seaweed, barnacles and mussels (El-Reedy, 2012, p.
385). Macro-fouling are of particular interest in this report and described in section 4.1.1.
4.1.1
Marine growth
On any kind of submersed member some kind of marine growth will develop over time. The
distribution will vary according to geographical location, water temperature, season, water
depth, ocean current, platform design, surface coating, structure material and platform
operation (Jusoh and Wolfram, 1996). Marine growth can be classied in three distinct
categories; hard growth, soft growth and long weed-like growth. In this thesis the most
important type is the hard growth which consists of primarily mussels and barnacles.
According to g. 4.1 on the next page the hard type of growth are mostly on the upper
region of structures. The thickness are greater than 10 mm down to about 30 meters of
depth. However from the gure it is clear that there is some presence of hard growth at
greater depths as well.
There is also great variation depending on location in the North Sea. According to g. 4.2
on page 28 the southern part and coastal areas of the North Sea are dominated by mussels,
whereas the northern part is not (Fevåg, 2012, p. 22).

4.2 Damage drivers
