4.2
Damage drivers
The reason to inspect structures is because they undergo damage processes which are
inherently random processes and cannot be completely accounted for in the design phase.
In this chapter the following questions will be sought to be answered
• What are the dierent sources of damage?
• How is damage detected?
4.2.1
Fatigue
Fatigue is dened as Failure of a metal under a repeated or otherwise varying load, which
never reaches a level sucient to cause a failure in a single application (Etube, 2001).
Fatigue analysis is not an exact science and due to the idealizations and approximations
the analysis will always be associated with a degree of uncertainty even when loading
environment is known. The reason to employ fatigue analysis is to make a prediction of
the expected fatigue life. Where fatigue life is dened as the time to develop cracks that are
both detectable and reparable but small enough to ensure that the structure can tolerate
it without catastrophic failures. Two dierent approaches have been developed to analyze
fatigue situations, called stress-cycle curves and fracture mechanics.
Stress-cycle curves
This approach is based on a stress-cycle (S-N) assessment of fatigue life. It has the ad-
vantage of being based on a single parameter, namely the hot spot stress, S, and yields a
fatigue life output, N, in terms of the cycles to failure. The basic formulation is given in
equation 4.1.
log10(N) = log10(K1) −m log10(S)
(4.1)
In practice mechanical parts are exposed to complex and often random load sequences. To
assess the expected life of such a part a number of steps are taken. First the complex loading
is reduced to a series of simple cyclic loading using rainow analysis then a histogram of
these loads is created to form a fatigue damage spectrum. The fatigue damage from each
of these cyclic loadings is found by S-N curves and the accumulated fatigue damage can
be assessed by using an algorithm called Miner's rule.
In summary the stress-cycle (S-N) curve approach is a simple tool for assessing the complex
damage processes of fatigue. However recent research has shown that the method should
be viewed with a healthy amount of scepticism, and more as a design guidance than an
absolute requirement (Etube, 2001).
==PAGE:46==
30
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Fracture mechanics
Fracture Mechanics (FM) analysis provides a complementary method for fatigue life as-
sessment of oshore structures. The analysis is particularly useful for aging installation
and life extension. Fracture mechanics dier from conventional stress-cycle curves in the
fact that it enables the analysis of defects, detected during inspections, and provides a
more reliable method of estimating remaining life (Stacey, Birkinshaw, and Sharp, 2008).
FM is used in the design stage but it is also used during operation to make important
decisions on inspection and repair strategies, for more information on these strategies see
section 4.6 on page 61. The FM models are based on empirical, semi-empirical models,
adapted at plate solutions and results based on nite element analysis. More information
can be found about the general method in (Etube, 2001).
4.2.2
Corrosion
Corrosion could be dened as a process where the chemical structure of a metal is changed
resulting in gradual deterioration by slowly degrading in a chemical oxidation-reduction
process. When a structure is subjected to fatigue loading as well as corrosion these pro-
cesses interact, and the deterioration is a result of the severity of the fatigue loading and
the rate of corrosion. This process is called corrosion fatigue (Etube, 2001, p. 24). The
main corrosion drivers of sub-sea parts, are listed below (Momber, 2011).
• micro-biologically inuenced corrosion
• galvanic corrosion
• crevice corrosion
• shallow pit corrosion
• uniform corrosion
When parts are under mechanical loads the following corrosion phenomena becomes ap-
parent as well.
• stress corrosion cracking
• corrosion fatigue
The relative metal thickness loss for a steel pile at dierent depths is shown in g. 4.3 on
the facing page. The two regions of high corrosion is the splash zone, above the mean high
tide and the upper part of the continuous submerged zone, just below the mean low tide
line.
==PAGE:47==
4.2 Damage drivers
31
Figure 4.3: Corrosion prole for steel pile after 5 years of exposure (El-Reedy, 2012, g.
6.6)
Oxidation-reduction
The standard corrosion driver is oxidation where ferrous materials form an oxide layer,
through a chemical process, which has inferior mechanical properties, compared to the
orignal material.
Fe →Fe2+ + 2e−
H2O + 1
2O2 + 2e−→2OH−
Fe2+ + 2OH−→Fe(OH)2
The iron(II) hydroxide produced in the above reaction is not very stable in the presence of
Fe2+ ions and will quickly oxidize to iron(III) hydroxide Fe(OH)3 which precipitate as a
reddish brown substance, the main constituent of rust. It is also noted that this corrosion
driver requires oxygen and thus is stronger in the oxygen rich Splash Zone.
Hydrogen embrittlement
Hydrogen embrittlement is the chemical process of hydrogen formation and propagation
in steel. It is mainly caused by cathodic over-protection. When hydrogen is formed near
the steel surface it diuses into the steel lattice structure. This decreases the ductility of
==PAGE:48==
32
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
the material and thus leads to embrittlement of the steel. Hydrogen embrittlement in high
strength steels is a complex process, however the general process can be described as the
following simple oxidation-reduction process. (Etube, 2001, p. 26)
Fe →Fe2+ + 2e−
(oxidation)
2H+ + 2e−→H2
(reduction)
Fe + 2H+ →Fe2+ + H2
(net reaction)
Three plausible theories has tried to explain the observed reduction of fatigue life induced
by hydrogen embrittlement, more information can be found in (Etube, 2001, p. 26).
Pitting corrosion
Defects in the steel surface can act as a anode which rapidly increases the corrosion locally
and creates a pit. This further increases the corrosion and can cause deep damages to the
structure which is very hard to visually identify.
4.2.3
Marine growth and fatigue
Marine organisms generally colonize a structure soon after installation. They grow rapidly
in the beginning, but growth tapers o after a few years. Marine growth causes an increase
in structural diameter, surface roughness and ow instability, thus increasing the environ-
mental loading, see g. 4.4 on the next page. However the weight is usually assumed to
be negligible. Marine growth exists in some form or another in all parts of the ocean and
must therefore be carefully considered when developing subsea solutions (API, 2002).
As seen in section 4.1.1 on page 26 the marine growth is most dense in the upper 30 meters,
which is also a critical region since waves further contribute to coating degradation (El-
Reedy, 2012).
==PAGE:49==
4.2 Damage drivers
33
Figure 4.4: The eect of marine growth on pipe diameter (API, 2002, p. 134)
4.2.4
Extreme weather
The loading on a structure due to extreme environmental events is a very important design
parameter. During design of a structure the extreme wave, wind and current loading are
considered, often with a return period of a 100 years. These events can be referred to as
the 100-year wave, wind or current. Survivability for a 1000 year or 10000 year return
period is also a requirement in some regions.
The main way of managing extreme weather is to design to the appropriate load criteria,
this requires a thorough analysis of the extreme events. Reduction in air gap can occur
through subsidence. Other means include providing an adequate air gap between the deck
of the platform and the water line, and also by managing marine growth.
The main means of managing the extreme weather hazard is to design to an appropriate
loading criterion, by providing an adequate air gap and by managing marine growth.
Reduction in the air gap can occur through subsidence (see section 4.2.6 on the next
page). The aging process of a structure can result in a reduction of its capacity to resist
extreme environmental events. Extreme weather can cause damage with little warning
causing fast mobilization of visual inspection systems important.
4.2.5
Accidental damage
Damage to the structure can happen due to accidental events while in operation, see
gure g. 4.5 on the following page. Examples include ship collisions and dropped objects.
However xed installations are designed to resist ship impacts by a criterion (usually
2500 ton at 2.5 m/s).
These accidents are fairly easy to identify since the cause of the damage is most likely
noticed and an inspection can be planned immediately after.
Repair may be required
depending on the level and location of the damage. The accumulation of damage over
==PAGE:50==
34
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Figure 4.5: A dented structure.
time, either minor in form and judged not to need repair or missed at the time of the event
can lead to an accumulated reduction in structural load carrying capacity. This damage in
the form of dents, cracks and bows can have a signicant eect on member capacity and
on the static capacity of tubular joints.
In (Galbraith and Sharp, 2007) the authors list the structural elements which are vulnerable
to accidental damage.
4.2.6
Geotechnical hazards
Foundation design includes the eects of both extreme environmental loads as well as con-
sideration of cyclic loadings. However there are considerable uncertainties in determination
of soil parameters and in calculating the capacity of piled foundations. There is also little
redundancy in piled foundations for jacket structures, with up to 6 piles driven through
the legs. Foundation failure can have serious consequences but few have been observed in
practice.
The primary means of managing geotechnical hazards are through the design and in-
stallation stage. This includes collecting site specic soil data and assessment of seabed
conditions. In-service inspection for seabed scour, settlement and subsidence are also im-
portant measures for managing these hazards. Seabed scour is the erosion of the sea oor
around foundations. Scour is formed due to the vortices created when water ows around
a vertical tube, see g. 4.6 on the next page. Seabed scour aects both lateral and axial
pile performance and capacity, but scour prediction is an uncertain science.
Sediment
transport studies may assist in dening scour design criteria, but local experience is the
best guide (El-Reedy, 2012).
==PAGE:51==
4.2 Damage drivers
35
Figure 4.6: The basic principle of seaoor scouring.
4.2.7
Protection systems
In this section dierent methods of protecting the structure from damage processes are
discussed.
Cathodic protection system
Cathodic Protection (CP) are the most widely used measure to prevent corrosion. Cathodic
protection can be achieved in two ways. The most common method involves attaching
sacricial anodes to the structure. Sacricial anodes are consumed during life and their
inspection is part of normal periodic platform inspections. Sacricial anodes has proven
to be a nuisance when inspecting structures using small ROVs as the tether tends to get
stuck in protruding anodes, see appendix A.1.
The other method involves applying a
cathodic potential or current to the structure using an external current generator. No
matter the method the general diculty is achieving uniform cathodic protection over the
entire structure. The actual levels of the cathodic potentials have considerable eect on the
fatigue crack propagation rates. The levels of cathodic protection can be categorized into
three levels, under-protection (or free corrosion), adequate protection and over-protection.
These levels are a function of the relative magnitudes of the protection potential and the
equilibrium potential for free corrosion of bare steel.
A cathodic protection system is used to reduce the rate of corrosion to a reasonable level
that allows the structure to retain it's design life. The systems works by applying a voltage
between a cathode and the structure in the range of around −800 to −1000 mV using a
Ag/AgCl reference.
The eect of letting the structure corrode in sea water under free corroding potentials
(around −600 to −700 mV) has been shown to reduce fatigue life of around two to three
times the time of fatigue life in air. However tests suggest that larger reductions in life oc-
curring at lower stresses, suggesting a possible eect of exposure time. Adequate protection
can produce fatigue life comparable to that of air (Etube, 2001, p. 30)
The inuence of over-protection is less conclusive. Some research suggests that very neg-
==PAGE:52==
36
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
ative cathodic potentials can cause hydrogen embrittlement and have a detrimental eect
on fatigue life in seawater (Etube, 2001). Tests also show that over-protection can result in
accelerated crack growth when compared with free corrosion and air fatigue crack growth.
Research on high-strength steels show that over-protection can increase crack growth rates
by as much as fty times when compared to crack growth rates in air.
Coating
The most popular passive corrosion protection method in practice is the application of
coating systems in order to separate the material to be protected from the corrosive en-
vironment. Coatings can be applied in several layers each with dierent properties. Two
major coating types are in use, non-metallic and metallic. The typical non-metallic coat-
ing is a combination of epoxies and polyurethane. The use of nonmetallic coatings is very
important, as they reduce the cathodic protection current demand for the protection of
the structure, which will reduce anode weight correspondingly. Metallic coating is an op-
tion for mechanically loaded sections. Coating thickness are usually up to a thickness of
500 µm (Momber, 2011; El-Reedy, 2012). Coatings have the disadventage of complicating
inspections of the underlying steel.
4.3
Inspection tasks
The goal of an inspection task is to detect if the geometry of interest has been damaged
by any of the damage processes described in section 4.2 on page 29.
According to (API, 2002)1 inspections can be divided into four dierent levels. For each
increasing level the complexity and scope of the inspection also increases. Naturally lower
inspection levels are of lower cost and will be performed more often.
The ISO 19902
standard provides more details about the specics of each inspection. In the following
section an overview of each of these inspection types will be provided.
4.3.1
Level I inspection
A level I inspection involves a subsea conrmation of the performance of the cathodic
protection system (e.g. by use of a drop-cell) and a general above-water visual survey.
Inspecting all structural members in the splash zone and above-water, with extra focus on
the condition of critical areas. If damage is detected and visual inspection cannot determine
the extent of the damage, Non-destructive Testing (NDT) is used. If the inspection indicate
that sub-sea damage is possible then a level II inspection should be performed as soon as
possible.
1The source is from the American petroleum institute but we assume that similar practices apply to
the North Sea
==PAGE:53==
4.3 Inspection tasks
37
4.3.2
Level II inspection
A level II inspection consists of a general underwater visual inspection. The inspection are
usually performed by divers or ROV. The following areas should be suciently covered:
• environmental or accidental loading damage
• scour or sea oor instability
• damage detectable in a visual swim-round survey
• design or construction defects
• presence of debris
• excessive marine growth
• measurement of cathodic potentials of critical pre-determined areas.
Detection of signicant structural damage during a level II inspection initiates a level III
inspection.
General visual inspections
General visual inspection of underwater installations with special attention to members,
braces and joints. The goal is to detect the presence of excessive corrosion, environmental or
accidental loading, fatigue damage, design or construction errors. General visual inspection
is only suitable for gross damage (e.g.
large deformations, severed members, missing
members or debris). The technology used is in most cases a simple camera.
Accidental damage
When accidental damage like the one described in section 4.2.5 on page 33 happens, an
inspection, to assess the magnitude of damage, can be required. Usually visual inspection
is deployed to inspect the extent of the damage. Members are inspected for dents and
bends. If an item is dropped then it the inspection will seek to locate it to decide whether
a more thorough inspection is required.
Scour
Scouring of piles can happen at all times however the extent is usually more severe after
storms. See section 4.2.6 on page 34 for more information. The scope of inspection is to
visually inspect the sea oor up to 7.5 m out for possible erosion and scouring or instability.
Inspection has to visually conrm if piles are exposed and if previous protected structural
members are exposed (El-Reedy, 2012).
==PAGE:54==
38
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Presence of anodes
As mentioned in section 4.2.7 on page 35 some structures employ a type of cathodic
protection involving sacricial anodes. These anodes are made of more reactive materials,
such as zinc or aluminum, as they are more electro-negative than the material that is to
be protected. One of the inspection tasks involve checking the status of these anodes, see
g. 4.7. These anodes are relatively heavy and are tted in large numbers, and will in
some cases have to be replaced with regular intervals. The inspection consists of a visual
survey to locate, count, grade and conrm the physical condition of the anodes. All sites
is inspected (both original and retrot) for loose, missing or damaged anodes. Potential
measurement and a detailed dimensional survey is to be taken on two anodes (El-Reedy,
2012).
Figure 4.7: A diver inspecting sacricial anodes.
Risers
Risers are one of the critical components of an oshore platform, see g. 4.8 on the next
page. Inspecting risers involves a visual inspection. Potential measurements are taken
between riser clamps. Record riser coating type and depth of termination. Inspect the
pipelines to the point of entry into the sea oor or 10 m from platform, whichever is closer.
Cassion
Some structures include a caisson, this type of structure is used for temporary storage of
production uids, see g. 4.9 on the facing page. Special care is taken to inspect the con-
ductors entering and exiting. Visual inspection of casings, casing clamps and intakes. Take
CP readings on clamps and casing mid-sections. General visual survey of the conductors,
with particular attention given to examination for conductor movement.
==PAGE:55==
4.3 Inspection tasks
39
Figure 4.8: The riser transport production uid from the seabed to the processing plat-
form.
Figure 4.9: A seabed caisson.
Potential of cathodic protection
One of the basic inspection tasks involve measuring the eectiveness of cathodic protection,
see section 4.2.7 on page 35. Cathodic protection is employed to mitigate the harmful
eects of corrosion, see section 4.2.2 on page 30. The inspection is done by making a
simple potential measurement, see g. 4.10 on the next page. This is achieved by making
an electrical contact between the surface of the structure under cathodic protection and
a reference electrode.
The most commonly used reference electrode is a silver / silver
chloride (Ag/AgCl) cell which produces a repeatable voltage when exposed to seawater.
The cell is then coupled with the surface of interest and the voltage between the ends
can be measured. Unprotected carbon steel will measure a potential approximately −0.62
to −0.57 V with respect to the Ag/AgCl cell. Anodes added to the structure shift the
potential negatively. The minimum accepted potential for bare steel in seawater is −0.8 V.
For more information refer to (Britton, 1999).
==PAGE:56==
40
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Figure 4.10: A diver measures the surface potential of a structure.
4.3.3
Level III inspection
A level III periodic inspection consists of an underwater close visual inspection (CVI)
of pre-determined areas of already detected or suspected damage. The areas is cleaned
thoroughly before inspection to allow close visual inspection. The areas should be deter-
mined by an engineering evaluation that are susceptible to structural damage but should
also be determined as areas where repeatable inspections can monitor integrity damage
over time. Wall thickness measurements can be performed at pre-determined locations to
assess the extent of corrosion damage. Flooded member detection (FDM) can in some
cases provide an alternative to CVI. Detection of signicant structural damage during a
level III inspection initiates a level IV inspection where visual inspection alone cannot
determine the extent of damage. The level IV inspection, if required, should be conducted
as soon as conditions permit. A level III and/or level IV inspection of fatigue-sensitive
joints and/or areas susceptible to cracking can be necessary to determine if damage has
occurred. Monitoring fatigue-sensitive joints, and/or reported crack-like indications, may
be an acceptable alternative to analytical verication.
Close visual inspection
Close visual inspection (CVI) dierentiate from GVI in the fact that the surface needs to
be cleaned for marine growth before the inspection can start. The goal of the inspection
is to detect damage that shows on a smaller scale, e.g. corrosion, visible cracks, dents,
abrasion and deformation.
4.3.4
Level IV inspection
A level IV inspection employs NDT techniques (see section 4.4.1 on page 42) to allow a
more thorough damage assessment than a level III inspection. Level IV inspections are
only performed on the most critical areas of the structure and usually allow a quantiable
measure of integrity damage over time. The areas inspected are usually pre-determined
==PAGE:57==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
41
areas that are susceptible to cracking or areas that have crack-like indications as identied
from a level III inspection.
Dimensional measurements
When GVI or CVI reveals damage of signicant factor the extent of damage might be
assessed by measurements. These measurements include marine growth thickness, scour
depth, dent size, member out-of-straightness, steel thickness, crack length or corrosion pit
size.
4.4
Inspection and monitoring technology
In this section dierent sensing and communication technologies will be described and put
in context of the proposed system.
For the oshore industry all technology must be proven reliable and well-tested under the
extreme conditions it may experience before it is implemented. Therefore when searching
and selecting technology, a redundant technology may be applied to ensure the reliability.
An overview of the technology covered is shown in g. 4.11 on the next page.
==PAGE:58==
42
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Figure 4.11: A overview of the technology covered
4.4.1
Non-destructive testing
In order to perform a level IV inspection, more sophisticated equipment is required. Non-
destructive Testing (NDT) equipment has been developed to enable inspections of critical
members, while in service, for defects that would otherwise not have been detected. A
large number of oshore structures consist of steel welded tubular joints, like shown on
g. 3.7 on page 17 the majority of which are positioned underwater. These nodal joints
can be highly stressed and subjected to cyclic loading, which makes them vulnerable to
fatigue failure. Fatigue failure is a gradual accumulating process, which means that if the
joints are inspected before fatigue failure, the damage might be mitigated (Abeele and
Goes, 2011). NDT technology comes with the promise of enabling such inspections and
has largely been successful. This section describes the most important NDT techniques.
Electromagnetic testing
Electromagnetic Testing (ET) techniques can all inherently detect and size a defect. The
main advantage of these techniques is that they do not require a surface contact and can
thus work through the protective coating (see section 4.2.7 on page 36) of the structures.
==PAGE:59==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
43
Figure 4.12: Working principle of Eddy Current Testing.
These methods requires a data analysis to determine the crack size. The drawback of these
techniques is that they cannot detect subsurface defects.
Currently there are three major techniques that have dierent levels of complexity
• Eddy Current Testing
• Alternating Current Field Testing
• Magnetic Particle Inspection
Eddy Current Testing
Eddy Current Testing (ECT) uses the principle of induction. By generating a high fre-
quency current in a coil and placing that coil close to the surface of a metallic object,
so-called `eddy-currents' are generated in the surface of the object. The inductive eect
of these eddy-currents can be measured as modulations of the current in the coil and pro-
duce useful information. ECT is generally used over small areas rather than for large-scale
testing. In g. 4.12 an illustration of the working principle is shown. Additionally a high
degree of operator skill is required. More information can be found in (AZO, 2013). The
main features of ECT are that it works through coating, the probe is rather small in the
millimeter scale, and that it can be used with a ROV, see (Nikhil, 2014). One of the
disadvantages is that there are a variety of dierent probes for dierent applications. For
datasheets of equipment refer to (SigmaCheck EC Conductivity Meter 2014; NORTEC 600
2014)
Alternating Current Field Testing
Alternating Current Field Measurement (ACFM) is a current perturbation method. The
method induces a current in the member, measuring the magnetic eld components and
relating them to the surface electric eld. The ACFM sensor consists of coils, sensitive to
changes in the magnetic elds components, which are parallel and normal to the crack.
ACFM works by making quantitative measurements of magnetic eld disturbances that
arise when an electric current is disturbed by the presence of a surface breaking crack.
==PAGE:60==
44
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Figure 4.13: The working principle of magnetic particle inspection. Magnetic particles
will be drawn to defects when a magnetic eld is applied to the ferromag-
netic surface.
Measurements of the magnetic eld components from coils in the probe are used along
with software algorithms to estimate the length and depth of the defect (Abeele and Goes,
2011).
The technique in its simplest form uses a hand held probe containing a uniform eld
induction system and two magnetic eld sensors. Software running on a PC is used to
control the instrument, display and analyse the data.
The main advantages of ACFM are that the technique works through coatings and no
calibration of equipment is required. Data is automatically saved for documentation and
analysis. ACFM is relatively insensitive to material property changes, so the technique
is ideal for inspecting welds. ACFM is also relatively insensitive to lifting of the probe,
allowing measurements through coatings and on rough surfaces. Depth sizing of defects
is based on theoretical models rather than on calibration.
Thus avoiding problems in
dierences between behavior of currents at slots and cracks, and dierences in material
properties between the calibration block and material around crack. ACFM usually allows
depth sizing of defects2 up to about 25 mm (Intech NDE, 2015).
Furthermore ACFM
performs better in response to variations in coating thickness (Nikhil, 2014). ACFM is the
primary NDT method for Maersk Oil and Gas, see appendix A.9 on page 189. A special
probe exists that can measure marine fouling, see (ACFM Marine Fouling Probe 2014).
The drawbacks are that defect dimensions need to be greater than 5 to 10 mm long and
0.5 mm deep and multiple defects reduce the ability to accurately size the depth of defects.
Magnetic Particle Inspection
Magnetic Particle Inspection (MPI) works by spraying the surface of a magnetic material
with magnetic particles and applying a magnetic eld. This causes the particles to stick to
discontinuities, see g. 4.13. It is a visualisation technique that indicates external cracks
on the structure and therefore performed primarily by experienced divers. This method is
used by Maersk as a second verication to ensure the location of the crack, see appendix A.9
on page 189.
2depending on probe type
==PAGE:61==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
45
Ultrasonic testing
Ultrasonic Testing (UT) techniques all relies on elastic wave propagation and detection.
The sound path in material without defects is straight and the wave velocity is constant, see
g. 4.14 for the basic working principle. Defects will cause refracted sound waves. These
refracted waves can be analyzed and information about the defect can then be inferred.
Ultrasonic inspections can both be performed by divers but also by ROVs, an especially
interesting development is the minimization of equipment that allows small observation
class robots to carry out simple ultrasonic inspections3. For more information refer to
(Abeele and Goes, 2011). In summary ultrasonic methods are used for the following tasks
in descending order of inspection complexity.
• Measurement of remaining metal thickness
• Detection of defects, even sub-surface defects and through coatings
• Sizing of defects
The advantage of ultrasonic testing techniques is that they can work through the protective
coatings (see section 4.2.7 on page 36) of the structure. UT can both detect surface defects
but also internal defects and far-surface defects, see g. 4.15. However they do require
calibration with known sample.
Figure 4.14: Basic working principle of ultrasonic metal thickness inspection4
Figure 4.15: An example of a far-surface defect
3A datasheet can be found in (MULTIPLE ECHO ULTRASONIC Digital Thickness Gauge 2014)
4Figure from Cygnus Instruments http://www.cygnus-instruments.com/us/technology/
==PAGE:62==
46
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Pulse-echo method (PE)
The pulse-echo technique is the most common method for oshore ultrasonic inspection. A
transducer and/or receiver probe is coupled acoustically with the test surface and emits a
sound pulse. The pulse will echo back to the receiver when it hits the back wall. Depending
on the received signal a defect can be identied and time of ight diraction (TOFD)
methods can estimate the crack size.
Phase-Array method (PA)
Phased array methods are a way of improving the ultrasonic inspections. The method is
able to modify the acoustic probe characteristics, e.g. by introducing time shifts in pulse
and echo. The method is thereby able to induce constructive and destructive interference.
These wave patterns can be analyzed to allow sophisticated inspections including, beam
steering, electronic scanning and dynamic depth focusing. More information can be found
in (Abeele and Goes, 2011; Satyanarayan et al., 2007).
Flooded member detection
The two predominant methods of Flooded Member Detection (FMD) are radio-graphic
and ultrasonic. The main drawback of FMD is late crack detection. Detection is only
possible when through-thickness cracking of the member occurs.
Radio-graphic Flooded Member Detection
Gamma Flooded Member Detection requires a gamma radiation source and a detector to
be xed to either end of a yoke that is attached to an ROV. The ROV pilot then positions
the yoke over the member and notes the reading count from the detector. This is compared
to an expected count that is calculated taking into account the absorption characteristics of
water between the yoke and the member, the member steel thickness and the air within the
member. If the member is ooded, the count is lower than expected due to the absorption
of the gamma radiation by the water and the member is agged as ooded. A datasheet
of ROV deployed radiographic FMD equipment can be seen in (Gamma Flooded Member
Detection 2014).
Ultrasonic Flooded Member Detection
Ultrasonic Flooded Member Detection uses an ultrasound probe that is acoustically cou-
pled to the outside of the cleaned member, see g. 4.17 on the next page.
A ooded
member can be detected by observing echoes that are received back at the probe. For an
air-lled member, the echo will be that of the back wall. For a water-lled member, the
ultrasound is transmitted through the water and a second echo will be generated at the
==PAGE:63==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
47
Figure 4.16: Typical setup for a ROV deployed FMD inspection (El-Reedy, 2012)
interface between the water and the back face of the tube, see g. 4.18 on the following
page. This back-face echo is an indication that the member is ooded. Datasheets of diver
or ROV deployed ultrasonic FMD equipment can be seen in (Flooded Member Detection
(FMD) 2014; Flooded Member Detection System (FMD) 2014).
Figure 4.17: Flooded member detection setup (El-Reedy, 2012)
==PAGE:64==
48
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
Figure 4.18: Flooded member signal (El-Reedy, 2012)
Cathodic potential measurements
Cathodic potential (see section 4.3.2 on page 39) measurements can be made with a device
called a bathycorrometer.
The challenge of measuring the potential is that usually a
ground reference is required which is above water.
However the device found in (H1
Buckleys Bathycorrometer 2014) is a hand-held diver-operated device. This should allow
the possibility of a device that is integrated in an AUV can be developed.
4.4.2
Surface cleaning technology
Regularly marine growth needs to be removed. The reasons include that the structure
must undergo close visual inspection or that the marine growth simply is too thick and
exceed design criteria. Dierent kinds of surface cleaning technology exists today, these
include blasting high pressure water, using a cavitation jet gun or simply using a rotating
brush. All of these methods have a relatively high power requirement and are not suited
for AUV deployment. For example the high pressure water must be supplied from the
topside.
4.4.3
Structural health monitoring
Structural Health Monitoring (SHM) is the concept of enabling continuous monitoring the
health and integrity of structures. The advantage of SHM is that it reduces the need for
subsea inspections while ensuring a high condence in safety. By carefully selecting loca-
tions for monitoring, the data obtained can enable the damage assessment to be targeted
to the area as soon as the damage occur. This reduces the time to repair and thus the cost
of repair (Stacey, Birkinshaw, and Sharp, 2008). Currently monitoring systems are mainly
installed on topsides due to far less complications (see appendix A.8 on page 188), e.g.
==PAGE:65==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
49
cabling on jacket legs breaking due to wave loads and the general high cost of mounting
and maintaining anything subsea.
The following monitoring technologies has been identied as interesting for subsea moni-
toring.
• Strain Monitoring
• Acoustic Emission Monitoring
• Natural Frequency Response Monitoring
• Fatigue Gauge Monitoring
• Continuous External Flooded Member Detection
All of the monitoring technologies are described in the following sections and a summary
is presented in table 4.1 on page 53.
Strain monitoring
Strain monitoring measures and monitors local strain variations in a way that information
of the local strain regime is obtained. This information is coupled with a nite element
analysis and the stress can be determined in the rest of the structure. Strain monitoring
are usually sought to be located away from steep stress gradients, e.g. near discontinuities
like a weld toe, mainly to reduce the averaging error introduced by uncertainty in the
gauge location (Etube, 2001).
Strain monitoring is used to measure the stress or loading of a particular part of a structure.
There are dierent kinds of strain measurement methods available. These include conven-
tional strain gauging, ber optics and stress probes. Measurement locations are usually
located away from stress concentrations in a way that the global structural loading can be
determined. The most common method of strain monitoring is with a conventional strain
gauge. The strain gauge is a device whose resistance varies proportionally with strain.
Since the strain in the strain gauge must be equal to the surface strain, the mounting of
the strain gauge is very important. Generally there are two methods of achieving this,
welding or adhesives. More information can be found in the following references (Ho-
mann, 2012; Agilent Technologies, 1999; Micro-Measurement, 1999; National Instruments,
1998; Kleckers and Gittins, 2004)
The limitations of strain monitoring include the following (May et al., 2009).
• Most strain monitoring methods can only measure stress variations and not absolute
stress levels
• The installation can be sensitive to environmental loading damage, especially the
cabling up the legs
• The gauges can drift over time and may require calibration.
==PAGE:66==
50
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
• The gauges location are sensitive, specically in areas with steep stress gradients
Datasheets for strain monitoring systems of the ber optics type can be found (Fiber Bragg
technology 2015) and for typical strain gauges in (Strain Gauges & Accessories for Strain
Measurement 2015).
Acoustic emission monitoring
The goal of Acoustic Emission (AE) monitoring is the detection of defects in materials. The
technique is primarily used for the detection of fatigue crack initiation and the monitoring
of crack propagation.
Acoustic Emission monitoring relies on the transient acoustic stress wave which occurs
when material changes. The principle of AE is to mount sensors in key locations to detect
the characteristics of the acoustic patterns when defects initiate and propagates.
The
system is usually deployed in areas of high risk and where inspections are dicult, costly
or unreliable. AE can eectively monitor crack initiation and propagation and can be
combined with strain gauges to correlate AE signals to stress levels. Due to attenuation
of the signal AE is used for local monitoring in the scale of meters.
AE systems have been used for over a decade in the oshore industry and is suitable for
monitoring of critical members. Due to relatively high costs AE has only been used for
critical applications.
The data obtained from AE systems is dicult to interpret and requires analysis by a
specialist.
Furthermore the system is prone to background noise which can aect the
accuracy of the system (May et al., 2009).
Natural frequency response monitoring
Natural Frequency Response Monitoring (NFRM) is system that continuously monitors
the changes in the platform natural frequencies. The changes allow the identication of
likely failed members. NFRM is used to measure pre- and post-eects of extreme events
such as storms, earthquakes, ship collisions and dropped objects. Furthermore it can detect
changes in foundation stiness.
The technique is based on the principle that the annual probability of failure in low redun-
dancy structures is governed by a few critical members. Accelerometers are placed in key
locations near these members both topside and subsea. The sensors measure the accelera-
tion response of the jacket structure to the environmental loading. These accelerations are
then transformed into the frequency domain and the natural frequencies can be monitored.
Usually a nite element analysis is performed to determine which mode shapes corresponds
to which frequencies. Major structural damage will change the structural stiness in one
or more modes. This will result in a reduction in the particular mode shape's natural
frequency. The change is then detected and reported. Changes in frequency caused by
soil stiness are reected in frequency changes in both platform directions. While changes
==PAGE:67==
4.4 Inspection and monitoring technology
51
in member stiness results in a frequency change in the direction which the member con-
tributes stiness.
In this way factors aecting the global structure, e.g.
water depth,
marine growth etc. can be discarded. A failure library can be created using nite element
analysis to assist in predicting the cause of the frequency shift, i.e. the most likely member
to have failed.
NFRM has been used in the oshore industry since the late 1970's on several platforms.
According to (May et al., 2009) there is at least twenty platforms with natural frequency
monitoring systems installed worldwide, with at least 10 systems in Europe.
The limitations of NFRM is that it is only sensitive enough to detect changes in frequency
of about 0.5 %. This limits the application of the method in detecting minor damage,
such as small defects. Generally the method is more dicult to apply in high-redundancy
jacket platforms, this is especially true for platforms with more than four legs. However it
may be possible to deploy a large number of sensors and link them in the time-domain to
monitor modal shapes as well as frequencies to identify damage (May et al., 2009).
Fatigue gauge
The fatigue gauge is a sensor that is designed to be mounted on a member, in order to
experience proportional cumulative fatigue loading. This allows a comprehensive record
of the local structural fatigue damage.
The gauge consists of a thin steel shim which is attached to the structure close to a critical
joint. During the cyclic loading action the sensor monitors a pre-placed fatigue crack in
the gauge. By monitoring the crack growth the sensor can correlate this to the fatigue
damage experienced by the joint. The availability of this very detailed fatigue data could
improve the inspection and maintenance planning signicantly.
Fatigue gauges has seen limited development in the oshore structure and has some in-
herent limitations. Especially that attachment to the structure is dicult. The placement
of the sensor has to be suciently close to the weld toe for correct response. Failure to
detect loss of adhesion may cause false negatives. Furthermore the sensors are required to
stay mounted on the structures and cannot be replaced when damaged (May et al., 2009).
Continuous external ooded member detection
Continuous External Flooded Member Detection has the purpose of detecting through
wall defects. It is similar to ooded member detection (see section 4.4.1 on page 46) but
is continuous and thus the lack of knowledge of member integrity between inspections are
eliminated.
A sensor is mounted outside a normally dry member. The sensor transmits a high frequency
acoustic signal through the member and measure the attenuation of the signal. If water
is present in the member then the signal is highly attenuated. When a change is detected
an alarm is sent to the operator, for example acoustically through the steel.
==PAGE:68==
52
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
The main limitation of the system is that the battery life is limited. It is possible to reduce
the requirements by only running the system periodically, e.g. every week. However battery
manufacturers have a limit of 10 years on battery reliability. Furthermore, members in the
splash zone are dicult to monitor, particularly if the sensors are too bulky (May et al.,
2009).

4.5
Inspection vehicles
To perform the broad spectrum of IMR tasks, the common vehicles used is inspection
divers, ROVs and AUVs. Each of these techniques has advantages and limitations and
depending on parameters eg. safety, campaign size, precision, operation time, depth, cost
among others, dierent system are chosen for the specic task.
Factors like industry
standards and governmental regulations will also dictate which system to use.
In the
following chapter, each system will be presented shortly.
4.5.1
Divers
Inspection divers are skilled and experienced professionals who can manage and execute In-
spection, Maintenance and Repair (IMR) operations on underwater structures, see g. 4.19
on the next page. Most of the equipment for NDT are designed and developed for pro-
fessional divers and thus most of the tasks requiring NDT revolves around divers. The
great advantage is that divers have an excellent awareness of the surroundings and great
tool dexterity. Divers works eciently have great agility around the structure. Inspection
divers have been used for many decades and with modern technology and special designed
diving systems, divers can work for extended periods of 10+ hour shift (Wilkins, 2006).
Depending on the inspection mission a team usually consists of 3-5 divers, see appendix A.1
on page 167.
Professional divers are limited by depth and mission length due to decompression sickness
and nitrogen narcosis. Normally the depth limit for recreational scuba divers are 40 meters
but professional divers can go as deep as 100 meters. Below this point saturation dives are
required and additional equipment are brought in (Wilkins, 2006).
Increased use of underwater remotely operated vehicles (ROVs) and autonomous underwa-
ter vehicles (AUV's) for routine or planned tasks means that saturation dives are becoming
less common, though complicated underwater tasks requiring complex manual actions re-
main the preserve of the deep-sea saturation diver.
Using divers require the highest safety standard because accidents can have severe conse-
quences for the individual. For the company it can result in a major disciplinary ne or
losing a contract. The usage of divers is exible and fairly straightforward, but as the plat-
forms are drilling deeper in harsher environment the safety may be compromised and as
stated in chapter 3 regulations are focusing more on removing the human from dangerous
work environments.
==PAGE:71==
4.5 Inspection vehicles
55
Figure 4.19: An inspection diver is welding on a structure
4.5.2
Remotely operated vehicle
Remote Operated Vehicles (ROVs) are unmanned, versatile and maneuverable vehicles
tethered to an inspection ship or topside. The tethered line provides power and commu-
nication to the ROV and from cameras mounted on the ROV, operators can maneuver
the robot. There are two ways a ROV can be tethered; 'free swimming' or 'garage'. Free
swimming submersibles are directly connected to the vessel or platform. Garaged ROV
operate from a submerged crate or 'tophat', see g. 4.22 on page 58, from were the tether
follows the seabed out to the ROV. This is used if the tether exceeds a length were the
'sail eect' aects the performance of the ROV. As the tether extends the ocean current
increases the drag on the line and thereby creating a pull on the ROV, thus immobilizing
it.
As a broad denitions ROV types can be divided according to their size in three categories.
In table 4.2 on the following page a brief overview of the large variety of ROVs followed
by a short explanation of each class.
• Mini class
• Observation class
• Work class

Mini-class
Mini class ROVs can be operated and handled by a single operator. These ROVs enable
frequent visual inspection or assisting larger ROVs and divers. A common mini class ROV
is the ultra light VideoRay that can be customized to minor tasks, see g. 4.20.
The
dimensions are small enough it can be carried by hand in a helicopter and lowered by one
person from topside or a vessel.
Figure 4.20: A customized VideoRay Pro 4, integrated with an Altimeter, Ship Hull
Crawler, Multi-Beam Sonar and Manipulator.
Observation class
Observation class ROVs covers both operations that the VideoRay can perform, but have
a broader range of attachments and applications. For example, a manipulator (multi-axis
arm), NDT equipment, cleaning tools or specialised scanning systems. They operate on
deeper water and can manipulate valves and gauges. These ROVs require a greater power
source and multiple people in deployment and operation, since the weight exceeds 30 kg.
ROVs are winched down either from topside or an inspection vessel.
Figure 4.21: A Saab Seaeye Tiger Rov with a 4 function manipulator.
Work class
Large work class ROVs have multiple manipulators, can carry a heavy payload and work
under extreme depth, seen in g. 4.22 on the following page. However they require several
==PAGE:74==
58
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
specially trained operators or pilots to control it, a large crane to deploy it from an in-
spection vessel or platform and an tether management system5. These robots are deployed
when new XTs are being build, digging trenches for pipes or handling heaving production
equipment.
Figure 4.22: A i-Tech Centurion-sp with dual multiaxis manipulators
Tether management
Tether management is very time consuming and can cause a huge problem with the ROV
system. Although modern ROVs have a high degree of mobility and systems to help the
operators, they require a very experienced pilots to avoid obstacles and move in three
dimensional space. The tether are easily tangled around the structure, equipment and
zinc anodes. If the ROV is tangled up, the cables have to be cut or managed by deployed
divers. The tether management can therefore limit the robot and the pilot in operation,
see appendix A.1 on page 167.
4.5.3
Autonomous underwater vehicles
This section describes the applications for autonomous underwater vehicles in terms of
oshore inspections. A comparison of dierent AUVs are made to highlight their capability
and specications.
Industry use
Autonomous systems have been a key component to optimise processes in many elds
and seen a great increase in the past years with the help of smaller sensors, better loca-
tion tracking, smarter algorithms embedded in aerial drones, self driving cars, production
facilities, spacecrafts etc. It is worth mentioning the dierence between automated and
autonomous systems dened in (Frost, 2010).
5Basically a winch which deploys the cable between the dry-side control unit and the subsea ROV
==PAGE:75==
4.5 Inspection vehicles
59
An automated system does not make choices for itself  it follows a script, albeit a poten-
tially sophisticated script, in which all possible courses of action have already been made.
If the system encounters an unplanned-for situation, it stops and waits for human help.
An autonomous system does make choices on its own. It tries to accomplish its objectives
locally, without human intervention, even when encountering uncertainty or unanticipated
events.
The challenges of introducing autonomous systems to a dangerous and unpredictable in-
dustry is not new. With the launch of the Deep Space 1 spacecraft in 1998 the autonomous
community reached a milestone, celebrating the rst demonstration of the feasibility of an
autonomous system in a remote environment, namely space. Even though, as seen in the
space industry, the pace of adoption has been relatively slow as well in the oshore industry
despite the many advantages.
The current use for an Underwater Autonomous Vehicle (AUV) involves a broad spectrum
of niche applications such as;
• Military
 Mine sweeping
 Reconnaissance
• Oceanography
 Marine life monitoring
 Ocean circulation
 Plate tectonic surveys
 Ocean oor surveys
• Oil and gas
 Pipeline inspections
 Structure scanning
Figure 4.23: A range of common AUVs
Many of these AUV designs are torpedo shaped and driven by either propellers or buoyancy
dierence called gliders, shown in g. 4.23.
They have simple monitoring tasks built
for covering long distances, but the torpedo form factor is a poor choice when distance
covering is not the main priority. Inspection of platforms and production equipment require
==PAGE:76==
60
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
more precise movements and manipulations, like the ROV (Triantafyllou, 2011). Only few
companies oer an AUV solution for subsea structures and equipment with the same
capabilities as an observation class ROV. An example of an AUV for structural inspection
applications is the Subsea 7 AIV, as seen in g. 4.24. The AIV is capable of undertaking
general visual inspection autonomously and with the vehicle Subsea 7 have envisioned a few
foresighted deployment scenarios in the near future, details in appendix C.2 on page 247.
A few companies also oer a tether-optional hybrid AUV, like the Double Eagle Sarov
Hybrid (Saab) 2014). This vehicle can be tethered if necessary and work as a ROV for
greater exibility.
According to (McLeod, 2010; Subsea7, 2012), implementing an intelligent and autonomous
underwater inspection robot can provide the following benets:
• Reducing support ship requirements
• Faster mobilization and demobilization
• Reduction in oshore crew and skills
• Lower risk of platform damage
• Greater inspection exibility
Figure 4.24: The Subsea 7 AIV during operation
In table 4.3 on the next page a list of specications for three commercially available au-
tonomous underwater inspection vehicles are listed.
A successful autonomous systems must carry out inspection task over a long period of
time without human intervention. Short period autonomy may even be worse than no
autonomy at all if the operators need to supervise the system and nominally the process.
Operators may end up spending more time than without autonomy. For a system like this
to work, communication for navigation and reliability are very important. The system must
act independently in order to continue the operation if unplanned circumstances occur.
Furthermore, oshore inspection is expensive, thus the system require high reliability and
low intervention by others systems (Chen et al., 2014; McLeod, 2010).

4.6
Inspection planning strategies
The detection of damage is almost entirely a function of when the hot spot is inspected.
Damage could exist for many years until discovered by the next programmed inspection.
Fatigue lives should not be inferred from inspection results unless the inspection intervals
are very short. (El-Reedy, 2012)
The following section describes the basics of inspection strategy. More information can be
found in (Straub et al., 2006).
==PAGE:78==
62
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
The general inspection strategies can be divided into three groups, xed interval (also
called prescriptive strategies, see section 4.6.1), partly risk-based inspection (qualitative
strategies, see section 4.6.2) and RBI strategies (quantitative risk-based strategies, see
section 4.6.3 on the next page).
4.6.1
Fixed interval
The Fixed Interval inspection is the traditional method of inspection where you choose
a schedule and inspect all joints in the structure, for example, every other year. By this
method no structural and risk analysis is required and can facilitate the planning of the
logistic aspects of inspection campaigns. This method of inspection make sure that within
the xed interval on the selected hotspots any problems will be spotted. Although it can
easily be adjusted to the available inspection capacities it may not be the most optimized
way since the need for inspection in joints varies with age, stress location and history,
accessibility etc.
Inspection interval
Depending on the exposure of a platform the guideline for inspection intervals in table 4.5
is used. The exposure level results of a risk assessment for every platform, depending on
a lot of variables particular for that platform. The highest exposure level is L1 while the
lowest is L3.
Table 4.5: Maximum inspection intervals for a common xed interval inspection program
(API, 2002).
Exposure Level
Inspection Level
Level I
Level II
Level III
Level IV
L1
Annual
3 years
5 years
Determined from level III inspec-
tion results
L2
Annual
5 years
10 years
Determined from level III inspec-
tion results
L3
Annual
5 years
Not required
Not required
Based on an interview with Maersk Oil & Gas they use an xed interval inspection program
of level IV inspections with an interval of 2 years for all their platforms. See appendix A.9
on page 189.
4.6.2
Partly risk-based inspection
In this qualitative or semi-quantitative risk-based strategy, the structure is prescribed into
separately groups based on quantitative risk indicators. A set of joints can hereby be a
primary, secondary or tertiary group, with a respective inspection interval. According to
(Straub et al., 2006) this method yield no major benets in reference to the xed interval
==PAGE:79==
4.6 Inspection planning strategies
63
method. It is argued that while strategies that are based on qualitative indicators are
preferable to completely prescriptive strategies, they do have similar disadvantages. For
example when fatigue deterioration are considered, the qualitative indicators provide poor
performance descriptions. The additional eort to gather the necessary indicators enabling
a full quantitative RBI strategy are relatively small.
4.6.3
Risk-based inspection
The objective of Risk Based Inspection (RBI) is to quantify the risks associated with
failure at a given hot spot. Hot spots are dened as certain points of interest, usually a
welding between two structural members. In risk management the risk are the likelihood
(probability) times the cost of an event (consequence) (Abeele and Goes, 2011).
The process of deterioration of oshore structures over time are in nature uncertain. The
uncertainties include operational conditions, environmental exposure, material characteris-
tics and manufacturing variation. The uncertainties originate in inherent physical random-
ness and in uncertainties and assumptions associated with the models used to predict the
performance of the oshore structures. In g. 4.25 a qualitative categorization of failure
events for oshore wind turbines, are plotted in terms of their probability and their conse-
quence. The eld coloring indicate the risk of the events. Events with high probability and
high consequence are categorized as high risk. By quantifying risks an optimal inspection
schedule can be calculated as to minimize the risks as low as reasonably possible. For
additional information about the RBI process refer to (Faber, Straub, and Goyet, 2003;
Straub et al., 2006)
Figure 4.25: Criticality matrix for oshore wind turbines. Green elds indicate low risk
events and red elds indicate a high risk eld (Abeele and Goes, 2011)
The benets of this strategy is the foundation upon which an inspector can access the
structures integrity and specify precisely where and how to inspect. The goal is to reduce
==PAGE:80==
64
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
the cost and time for inspection campaigns while preserving the same or reducing the
risk (Straub et al., 2006). The process from assessing the risks to obtaining an optimal
inspection program can be visualized by the owchart in g. 4.26. The process starts with
a failure mode assessment and a qualitative experience-based indication of consequence
and probablity like in g. 4.25 on page 63. The components responsible for the failures
are then identied and the predicted damage behaviour can be analyzed by the methods
briey introduced in section 4.2 on page 29. The optimal inspection schedule can then be
found by a optimization algorithm that minimizes the total cost while constrained to an
acceptable level of risk.
Figure 4.26: Risk assessment owchart (Abeele and Goes, 2011)
Hazard rate
The hazard rate for most engineering equipment can be characterized by a reliability bath
tub curve, as seen in g. 4.27 on the facing page. The curve is divided into three regions.
The rst part corresponds to the start of life and has an increased hazard rate due to
variations in material properties, manufacturing defects, poor design choices and errors in
installation and operation. As a result the weakest assets fail in this region. When the
failures are corrected the hazard rate falls as seen on the gure. Following this initial poor
performance region is the useful life region with general good performance. The last region
corresponds to the end-of-life region of the asset. The failures in this region are primarily
due to age or degradation. g. 4.27 on the next page illustrates the benets of risk-based
==PAGE:81==
4.6 Inspection planning strategies
65
inspection programs as focusing inspection eorts when hazard rate is high and relaxing
the inspection eorts when the hazard rate is low.
Figure 4.27: Engineering reliability bathtub curve (Abeele and Goes, 2011)
Probability of detection and probability of sizing
Using any inspection techniques the statistical concept of probability of inspection are im-
portant. The concept relates to the reliability of the inspection. Inspections should reveal
the location of a crack and the length and depth of the crack. Probability of Detection
(POD) is the statistical measure of the success of an inspection, and the Probability of
Sizing (POS) relates to the techniques sizing accuracy. In g. 4.28 on the following page
the ideal and realistic behaviour of POD and POS are illustrated.
In sizing, as seen from the POS curve, techniques tend to underestimate the aw sizes.
This can have serious consequences if the POS is not taken into account. The diculty of
NDT inspection is illustrated in g. 4.28 on the next page.
For the POD the problem is, while the NDT technique indicates that a crack is present,
in reality the crack might not be present. This leads to a false positive. Furthermore
since most techniques has a lower limit of the aw sizes that can reliably detect, inspectors
have to assume that on all negative inspections are aws that are just below the minimum
detectable aw size. In g. 4.29 on the following page a study from Maersk in establishing
POD curves for their probabilistic modelling is shown. From the gure it is seen that with
90 % condence detect defects with a depth of about 4 mm.
As a general rule, the higher the POD the more sophisticated technology and time is
==PAGE:82==
66
Inspection and monitoring of subsea oshore structures
(a) Probability of Detection depending on aw
size.
(b) Probability of Sizing depending on aw size.
Figure 4.28: Statistical measures of reliability engineering (Abeele and Goes, 2011)
required. Therefore an increase in POD will result in a longer inspection interval, if the
industry term As Low As Reasonably Possible (ALARP) is followed.
Figure 4.29: Probability of Detection curves for dierent NDT methods. The curves are
combined in a mastert curve (Tychsen, 2008)
4.7
Technological opportunities
The implementation of a risk-based inspection strategy facilitate direct reduced total ex-
pected costs, and in addition also leads to a deeper understanding of the structural weak
points. In some cases this lead to better mitigation measures which are more ecient than
==PAGE:83==
4.7 Technological opportunities
67
inspections. Furthermore it will also be benecial to perform a RBI study already in the
design phase of the structure and thus actively design joints for a cost optimal inspection
strategy (Straub et al., 2006).
However RBI studies rely entirely on the ability to assess risks on fatigue aected joints.
This assessment depends on the validity of the models used to predict damage. As dis-
cussed in section 4.2 on page 29 these damage processes are inherently physical random
processes. In order to mitigate this randomness, most standards (API, 2002) recommend
safety factors. These safety factors will lead to increased costs, and not increased safety,
when they are applied to non-governing failure modes.
Another point is the fact that damage will only be detected when inspections are performed.
With relatively high costs of inspection, the current push is to only inspect when absolutely
necessary. With this strategy damage can potentially go unnoticed for extended periods,
this leads to increased repair costs.
In this light an inspection and monitoring system which improves the structural integrity
management of the oshore industry has the following requirements
• Provide actual measurements of the fatigue damage
• Can be deployed quickly after a major event that increases the probability of early
damage detection
• Should either has a high probability of detecting damage or be performing inspection
often
4.7.1
Inspection technology opportunities
In table 4.6 on the following page all the dierent levels of inspections are listed along with
their associated technologies. The enabling vehicle is marked for each technology. It is
concluded that divers still dominate inspections in a way that if a structure requires a level
IV inspection then the only option is divers. On the other hand ROVs can handle most
of the required inspection tasks. AUVs has seen limited use in industry so far and one of
the reasons can be the value is not high enough, provided by only enabling a small part of
the inspection requirements to be automated. A system which involves AUVs should yield
more value to operators than currently the case.

(May et al., 2009, p. 53) has identied the following general limitations for current SI

• Robust sensors: Underwater deployment of sensors can be damaged from wave
action, marine growth and other mechanisms.

Chapter 5
Autonomous Inspection and
Monitoring System (AIM system)
The following two chapters will present the design of our AIM system, an autonomous
scalable system capable of collecting and replacing structurally mounted monitoring units
while performing lighter inspection operations.
The AIM system system is developed based on the market opportunities in section 3.8 on
page 24 and the technological opportunities in section 4.7 on page 66. A set of requirements
has been produced based on the previous chapters. The functions of the system are dened
and the means to these functions are generated. From these means several distinct concepts
are created. These concepts are evaluated against the requirements and the best performing
concept is selected to frame the system concept.
Each part of the system is discussed, including the AUV, the logistics and the technical
considerations for the monitoring system. The technological readiness for implementing
the AIM system is assessed and the risk of other emerging systems are discussed. Lastly
the solution space is framed for the development of a monitoring unit.
==PAGE:88==
72
Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system)
5.1
Functions
The problem driving this design is enabling cost-eective subsea inspection and monitoring
of structures. In this design two actors are involved, the installation actor, which is the
actor that installs the system on the structure and the collection actor, which collects data
and interacts with the monitoring unit after the system has been installed.
The main functions of the system are shown in g. 5.1. The main functions are associated
with a set of sub-functions. These sub-functions are then divided into dierent colors.
The white sub-functions are discussed in this chapter, the blue sub-functions are specic
for the monitoring unit that will be discussed in chapter 6 on page 93. The black colored
sub-functions are black-boxed. We made the decision to exclude the actual installation
process between the decision to install sensors and the mounting of the sensors on the
structure. The reasons were that areas that are to be monitored requires a thorough
cleaning for marine growth1 before any installation can take place. This means that this
system will have to rely on the established infrastructure on this specic point.

5.3
Conceptualization
The approach for generating concepts involves producing, for each function, a set of means
of achieving that function. For example one function is move robot and personnel to
structure, this function can, for an oshore structure, only realistically be accomplished
in four dierent ways. Either by helicopter, supply ship, inspection ship or the robot can
be permanently deployed on the platform. The set of system functions (from g. 5.1 on
==PAGE:90==
74
Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system)

5.4
Selection and evaluation of concepts
The concepts found in the previous section are evaluated and selected in this section. This
is based on a quick assessment of the most important criteria. A decision matrix is used
for scoring the concepts in order to identify the best performing concept (Ullman, 2003).
The relative importance of the criterias are scored on an simple scale from 1-5. This score
is then converted into a relative weighing using the formula (5.1) where i is the criteria
index.
Wi =
xi
Pn
i=1 xi
· 100
(5.1)
Each concepts are scored on the requirements in relation to a reference concept. The scoring
is done by evaluating the potential performance of the concept in relation to the criteria and
the reference. −1 symbolizes the concept will perform worse than the reference in regards
to the criteria, 0 is the same/equalized expected performance and 1 is a better expected
performance. The weighted total can then be calculated as a sum product between the
concept row and the relative weighing. The results are listed in table 5.1 on the next page.
We note that concept 7: Basket dump is performing signicantly better than the other
concepts. It is also noted that the reference concept performs worse than almost all of the

5.5
Concept: Basket dump
The system concept is illustrated in g. 5.3. In this concept we have a few external actors,
these are introduced below.
• Mission control
• Supply ship
• Inspection ship
The mission control is tasked with the remote supervision of the system. The location of
the mission control is exible and can be located onshore, on a platform or on a vessel.
The supply ship is the usual supply ship that brings goods to the platform. The main
function is to transport the system to and from the platform. Lastly the inspection ship
is required to install the monitoring units.
The basic functionality of the system is initiated with the basket containing an AUV is
prepared onshore. It is then shipped on a supply ship to the platform and dumped in
the water at a predened location. The AUV performs the tasks required and returns
to the basket where it waits for the supply ship to picks it up and return to land. The
functionality of the system is described in greater detail in section 5.5.2 on the next page.
The contents of the system will now be described more in-depth below.

==PAGE:93==
5.5 Concept: Basket dump
77
5.5.1
System contents
The system is contained in the system container. The system container includes three
parts; a buoy, a basket and an anchor, The buoy and the basket are connected with a
wire that has several electrical connections and the basket and anchor are connected with
strong mechanical link. The anchor serves the purpose of ensuring that the basket stays
around the same area.
Buoy
The buoy contains a light for signalling to the supply ship crew members and long-range
wireless electromagnetic communication to the mission control crew. The light has three
modes with dierent light signals for each, rst is that the system is ready to deploy (AUV
is in the basket), second is that a mission is underway (indicating that the AUV is not in
the basket), the third is that the mission is complete and the system container is ready to
be retrieved.
Basket
The basket contains an autonomous inspection vehicle, AUV, a magazine of tested and
ready sensor units, a magazine for used sensor units, a large battery for powering all
electronics and recharging the AUV, an entry point for docking the AUV, a protective
casing, necessary localization AUV technology, e.g. an acoustic modem for localization
purposes and/or lights.
Autonomous underwater vehicle
The autonomous underwater vehicle (AUV ) has a camera (with adequate spectrum sensi-
tivity for maximum underwater contrast), ood-lights for illumination, sucient monitor-
ing unit tools, an acoustic communication modem, a near-eld electromagnetic communi-
cation device, e.g. Near-Field Communication (NFC) (a few meters distance scale).

Figure 5.6: The deploy system function
g. 5.6 shows the process of deploying the system. The vessel crew dumps the system
container in the water within a reasonable radius (in the order of tens of meters) of the
GPS coordinate given.
The system container descends near the seabed.
The system
container then deploys and the anchor is automatically ejected and xes the basket to the
seabed. The buoy is ejected and oats to the surface where it emits a light that signals that
the system is deployed. This signal can be detected by the supply vessel crew but also the
platform crew. The buoy acts as an indicator for the status of the AUV and the location
of the basket. The buoy then establishes a communication link with the mission control,
to allow status monitoring of the basket and the AUV. When the basket is successfully
deployed the buoy sends a signal to the mission control conrming the status.
Collect data
g. 5.7 on the next page shows the process of the data collection mission. The mission
control sends a signal to the buoy that a data collection mission is to be started. The
basket then opens up and the AUV leaves the basket. The buoy signals mission control
after a successful initiation. After successfully leaving the AUV emits an acoustic wake-up
call to the sensors. These now start providing localization information to assist the AUV
in navigation. The AUV navigates to the sensors and starts to interact with these. When
nished the AUV navigates back to the basket and docks. When docking is successful the
buoy signals the mission control of a successful mission. During the mission the AUV uses

Retrieve system
Figure 5.9: The retrieve system function.
g. 5.9 shows the process of retrieving the system. The system can be extracted by two
methods, the rst being that the system has nished its job and the AUV has returned
safely to the basket. The buoy then emits a light that signals that the system is ready to be
collected. The other method is that the onshore crew signals to the buoy that the mission
is to be cancelled. The basket then emits a long-range acoustic signal to the AUV. The
AUV then returns to the basket, the buoy sends a long-range signal back to the onshore
crew that the mission is aborted and the system is now ready to be collected.
5.5.3
System value propositions
In the previous sections the functionality of the system has been established. In this system
the value of the system will be discussed. The system provides value to oshore operators
in two overall forms, within General Visual Inspection and Structural Health Monitoring
(SHM).
The details of General Visual Inspection are discussed in section 4.3 on page 36 and the
value propositions for this task is as follows
• Allowing a exible inspection schedule
• Enabling quick inspections after major incidents
• Minimizing inspection vessel time by focusing on complex tasks instead of repetitive
visual inspection.
==PAGE:98==
82
Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system)
However the greatest value proposition is enabling underwater SHM. Below the value
propositions for SHM are divided into the value created for the monitored platforms, the
platforms that will be built in the future and platforms that are similar to the monitored
platforms. The benets are listed after the assumption that the risk of failure is lowered
after the system has been implemented, since the platforms has been designed with safety
factors in mind. If this assumption hold then inspection and maintenance work can be
reduced. However, if the opposite is true, that the risk set too low, then this information
may prove to be even more valuable, as mitigation measures can be applied before a failure
occur.
Monitored platforms
• Increased condence in risk assessment (risk decrease)
 Reduction in number of hot spots
 Increase in inspection frequency
 More reliable hot spot documentation (Reduced insurance premium)
 Increase in chance to use the right mitigation option (repair or not?)
• Higher condence in platform integrity
 Can resist more extreme weather (less need for personnel evacuation)
• Early detection of large damage
Future platforms
• Reliable knowledge of platform performance
 Material is allocated where it is needed (lower material cost in manufacturing)
 Joints can be designed to resist failures better (lower stress concentration, in-
crease in fatigue life)
• Understanding of platform behaviour subjected to extreme weather (design for ex-
treme weather)
Similar platforms
• Some of the benets for the current platform can be carried over.
