5.6
Technological readiness
This section describes the technological readiness of the novel system concept. A system
that performs the functions described in this chapter does not exist, however all of the
features and technologies utilized in this concept are found in other kinds of systems.
==PAGE:101==
5.6 Technological readiness
85
5.6.1
Autonomous navigation
Autonomous navigation systems are crucial for performing the main functions of the pro-
posed system. The issues of reliable underwater navigation boils down to the heavy at-
tenuation of GPS and radio signals. This leads to the fact that AUVs, after deployment,
are mostly on their own. Navigation are achieved by dead reckoning, pre-placed beacons
and/or geophysical techniques. The technologies are outlined below. (Paull et al., 2014)
discusses the dierent technologies more in depth and (Chen et al., 2013) categorizes the
localization technology used by 35 dierent AUVs.
• Dead Reckoning: Inertial navigation uses accelerometers and gyroscopes for in-
creased accuracy to propagate the current state. Nevertheless, all of the methods in
this category have position error growth that is unbounded.
• Acoustic transponders and modems: Techniques in this category are based on
measuring the time of ight (TOF) of signals from pre-installed acoustic beacons or
modems to perform navigation.
• Geophysical: Uses external environmental information as references for navigation
e.g. seaoor, platform structure and depth measurements. This must be done with
sensors.
It is noted that dead reckoning and geophysical navigation relies on intrinsic technology
while acoustic transponders and modems relies on both AUV intrinsic technology but
also on extrinsic technology, such as beacons that are installed at known locations. An-
other take-away is that none of the technologies are sucient by themselves. For optimal
navigation information fusion of several sensors are required. In the proposed system the
monitoring units are utilized as acoustic transponders that are mounted in known locations
to assist the navigation of the AUV.
5.6.2
Docking in basket
In (Hobson et al., 2007) the authors shows an AUV that docks in a cone-shaped docking
module in ocean conditions using a Ultra-Short Base Line (USBL) sonar transceiver. While
(Bellingham et al., 2001) shows a homing system that employs electromagnetic eld lines
to guide to AUV into a single entry point in the dock. (Tang, Yu, and Nie, 2015) describes
an experimental setup where an AUV docks in a cone-shaped docking module with an
optical beacon.
The authors in (Wu et al., 2014) investigates the issues in regards to
the hydrodynamics of a cone-shaped dock. The authors suggest optimal geometry for the
cone dock. These articles illustrate the amount of work that has been done to allow the
docking of an AUV to be successful, and describe the torpedo-shaped AUV type. However
in (Jamieson, Wilson, et al., 2015) the authors describe a dock for hover-capable AUVs
which is similar to the dock to the basket in this concept.
==PAGE:102==
86
Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system)
5.6.3
Passive structure interaction
In (Palomeras et al., 2014) the authors show a method for docking an AUV to a passive
interface to enable the performance of a simple task such as grabbing and turning a handle.
In the setup described the AUV utilize only forward thrust to connect with the interface,
however the setup was performed in a pool and not in the ocean. The task is similar in
complexity to the task of replacing sensors in the proposed system.
5.6.4
Underwater communication
As investigating the opportunities for monitoring and autonomous systems, wireless com-
munication is an essential function for the system, whether the communication is between
several monitoring units or the navigation of the AUV. Present underwater communication
systems uses either radio frequency (RF) waves, optical waves, or acoustic waves. Each of
these techniques has advantages and limitations. The two main challenges in underwater
communication are (Benson et al., 2010)
• Range
• Data rate
The three major forms of underwater communication are discussed along with the advan-
tages and drawbacks in table 5.3.

Radio frequency waves
Radio frequency waves are essential in terrestrial communication, but meets limitations
due to the conducting nature of salt water. As the waves propagate through water the
waves cycle between the electric and magnetic eld experiencing a strong attenuation.
Attenuation of radio waves increases when conductivity of the water and the frequency of
the RF waves increase.
==PAGE:103==
5.6 Technological readiness
87
Pure water is an electric insulator, however in the real world, ocean water contains dissolved
salts and other particles, which makes sea water a partial conductor. Increasing water
conductivity leads to greater attenuation of radio waves that pass through the water. The
attenuation α of the RF waves can be calculated using the following formula
α = 0.0173
p
fσ
(5.5)
Where f is the wave frequency and σ is the water conductivity. Water conductivity depends
on the salinity of the water. This means that high water salinity and high wave frequency
increases wave attenuation. So in order to communicate at any reasonable distance, very
low communication frequencies are necessary.
However very low frequencies require a
large antenna to capture the signal of the larger wavelength. Another inuencing factor
is the transmission loss of the signal. The transmission loss can be described by Friis'
transmission equation (Sidén and Nilsson, 2010).
Pr
Pt
= GtGr
 λ
4πR
2
(5.6)
Where the fraction Pr
Pt is the power ratio of the transmitting antenna to the receiving
antenna. If the goal is to minimize power loss then this fraction needs to come as close
to 1 as possible. Gt and Gr are antenna gains, λ is the wave frequency, related to the
frequency band chosen and R is the distance between the antennas. In order to minimize
power loss a high frequency is desirable. The conclusion is that a trade-o between range,
bandwidth and power loss is required.
table 5.4 provides an indication of the current maximum obtainable ranges, data rates and
suggested applications. More information is found in (Benson et al., 2010)

Optical waves
Optical waves are electromagnetic waves with wavelengths between 400 nm (blue light)
and 700 nm (red light). Optical waves are generally limited to short distances because
of severe water absorption at the optical frequency band and strong backscatter from
suspended particles. Sea water is least absorptive around the 400 to 500 nm band (Benson
et al., 2010), which is the blue-green region of the visible light spectrum. However the
more severe limitation is the backscattering of optical waves. The amount of scattering
depends on the turbidity of the water. Thus optical waves are great in clear waters but
unusable when in turbid water (Anguita et al., 2011). The main drawback of using optical
waves is that the performance is highly dependent on the environment, and thus if the
environment changes signicantly the optical waves are usually a poor choice.
Acoustic waves
Acoustic underwater communication uses acoustic waves to transmit information. Acoustic
waves are caused by variations of pressure in a uid. Water has a much greater density than
air and thus acoustic waves travel four to ve times faster in water than in air (travelling
around 1500 m/s in water). Acoustic waves has the advantages that the attenuation of
the signal is relatively low. However acoustic waves suer from absorption loss, spreading
loss, ambient noise and severe multipath (Benson et al., 2010). In general acoustic waves
provide long transmission ranges but are limited by low data rates and interference.
Node Wake-up call
One of the issues of acoustic communication is the power usage of the components. A way
of reducing the total energy used by the system is by implementing a low-power sleep mode
which listens for a wake-up call. This method is described in (Sanchez et al., 2011). This
way sensor units can provide localization information only when required. The technology
can be adapted such that selective wake-up calls can be sent to specic sensor nodes,
allowing only the necessary sensors to be active. When the localization information is no
longer required sensors can either be turned inactive by a sleep call or be on a timer.
==PAGE:105==
5.7 Failure modes
89
Sensor-AUV communication
In the proposed system concept two modes of communication are utilized. There is an
acoustic communication link between monitoring units and the AUV for localization and
navigation purposes. Furthermore near-eld radio frequency is utilized when the AUV is
suciently close to the monitoring units.
As discussed in section 5.6.1 on page 85, navigation is essential for this system. Thus it is
not feasible to let the AUV only rely on internal sensor systems when the concept include
monitoring units that are positioned in known locations. By including an acoustic modem
in the monitoring units another mode of localization for the AUV is enabled.

5.8
Development risks
This section describes technologies which will make the whole system or parts of it obsolete
and thus pose a risk to the success of the system.
Three dierent technology scenarios has been identied.
• Wireless underwater sensor networks
• Wired underwater sensor network
==PAGE:107==
5.8 Development risks
91
• Perfect damage models
Each one of these is discussed more in detail below.
5.8.1
Wireless underwater sensor networks
In the case that sensor networks become so technologically advanced that they allow wire-
less subsea transfer at so low power consumption that battery depletion rate is not an
issue. This implies that
• Nodes can transfer data wirelessly over a long range
• Nodes can withstand long-term exposure to ocean
• Nodes have large batteries or employs energy harvesting
The issues and limitations of underwater sensor networks are discussed in the following
references (Akyildiz, Pompili, and Melodia, 2004; Lanbo, Shengli, and Jun-Hong, 2008;
Akyildiz, Pompili, and Melodia, 2005; Heidemann, Stojanovic, and Zorzi, 2011; Vasilescu,
Detweiler, and Rus, 2007). The conclusion is that the technology is not ready yet. Even
if sensor nodes can transfer the required data, then there is still the limitation of power.
Power consumption of the required components are not on a level at the moment that
allows the required battery pack to be small enough. In addition the amount of power and
the size of energy harvesting devices are not on a feasible level yet.
5.8.2
Wired underwater sensor network
A wired sensor system does not have the problems of the wireless sensor networks. At rst
a wired sensor network might seem like an obvious choice. Data can be collected in real
time and the cabling provides power to the sensors. However wired sensor networks have
a number of limitations that makes them unfeasible for underwater applications. First of
all the installation of wired sensor networks are a major undertaking. All sensors that are
mounted will obviously have a cable from the unit to the topside, and if a lot of sensors
are mounted then the problem grows rapidly. This problem can be reduced by combining
cables. However if a cable breaks a whole group of sensors will be lost. Furthermore the
attachment of cables to steel pipes are not straight forward. Cables on the outside of pipes
are exposed to cyclic wind and wave loads, while also prone to damage caused by boats.
In essence the installation, maintenance and lack of redundancy makes the management
of a wired underwater sensor network very dicult.
5.8.3
Perfect damage models
If the development of damage models improve signicantly then the need for measuring
data might be reduced. In this case the requirement for inspections will also be reduced.
The long-term progress of models is dicult to predict. However as discussed in section 4.2
on page 29 the damage drivers of oshore structures are based on physical processes that
==PAGE:108==
92
Autonomous Inspection and Monitoring System (AIM system)
are inherently random. This makes it hard, if not impossible, to predict the exact fatigue
state of every member on the structure.
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 6
Monitoring concept
Based on the opportunities discussed section 3.8 on page 24 and section 4.7 on page 66
the AIM system depends on a working independent monitoring unit, recording relevant
data on the structure. The data collection is the primary value the system concept oers
with the purpose of enabling ecient and optimized inspections and is therefore essential.
The chapter will introduce the basic concept of the monitoring unit and the following will
describe the concept selection and the monitoring designs four interfaces in detail and how
this concept is technologically feasible.
The approach to solve the monitoring challenge is to determine the core requirements of the
unit and mapping the mechanical, physical and technical limitations while assessing the use
and lifespan of the unit while submerged. This is accomplished by accessing the industry's
advancement in technologies and draw on established knowledge from the scientic eld,
with the goal of creating a plausible concept that could be produced in the near future.
==PAGE:110==
94
Monitoring concept
6.1
Introduction of the monitoring unit
This section will give a basic introduction of the fundamentals of the monitoring unit.
Subsequent sections will discuss more in detail the design choices.
The core value proposition of the monitoring units is the gathering of strain, acceleration
and gyroscopic data from subsea structures. A basic sketch of the idea can be seen in
g. 6.1. In this sketch there are two interfaces, one with respect to the structure and one
with respect to collecting data. For the structure the interface is not consistent as the
diameter of the members can vary greatly1. This means that it will be quite dicult to
design a single interface that will robustly connect to the varying member diameters.
Figure 6.1: Simple data collection diagram for the monitoring unit
Drawbacks of the simple design in g. 6.1
• Dicult to ensure robust interface between the structure and monitoring unit caused
by varying member diameters
• Limitations to the choice of interface technology for the strain gauge
1According to Force Technology from around Ø600 mm to Ø6000 mm.
==PAGE:111==
6.2 Main functions and requirements
95
Figure 6.2: Basic diagram of the expanded concept
Introducing adapter
Strain measurements are usually done by a strain gauge which is either applied with
adhesive or welded to the surface. Because of this the idea dictates the interface should
be permanent and this aects the interchangeability. For collecting and transferring data
the monitoring unit require energy from an integrated battery, therefore limiting it to a
one-time use scenario. Ideas for replacing or recharging the battery have proven to be
impractical and very unlikely. In order to mitigate these drawbacks of the monitoring
unit, a simple adapter part were introduced with an integrated strain gauge.
Some of the advantages of adding an adapter:
• Controlled interface geometry between adapter and sensor
• Allow replacement of the sensor for battery change and system upgrades
• Easier to automate and ensure interface engagement and disengagement
A basic sketch of the concept is seen in g. 6.2. The adapter will be permanently mounted
on the structure, thus allowing the transformation of varying surface characteristics to a
controlled interface for the sensor replacement. With the perspective of battery recharging
the design enables sensor replacement with a recommended 12 month data interval (see
appendix A.8 on page 188).
6.2
Main functions and requirements
The monitoring unit has two main functions; installation on the structure and interaction
with actor. Each of these two main functions has a set of sub-functions. The sub-functions
==PAGE:112==
96
Monitoring concept
are listed below.
• Installation of the monitoring unit
 Cleaning of location
 Placement of the monitoring unit on structure
 Secure mounting of the monitoring unit
• Monitoring unit interaction
 Monitoring unit localization
 Data transmission of measured data
 Recharging of the monitoring unit
 End-of-life
The installation of the monitoring unit requires the site of installation to be cleaned of
marine growth before the monitoring unit is placed on the structure and should be mounted
securely.
The monitoring unit interaction function can be divided into several sub functions. The
monitoring unit must be localized, then the transfer of the measured data must be allowed
by the system. The ability to recharge the monitoring unit must be considered and the
end of life function has to be designed.
One of the main failure modes of this concept is expected to be the monitoring unit
disconnecting from the structure. Thus the concept should aim to maximize the achievable
holding force between the monitoring unit and the structure. The next section will take
a step back and identify the requirements and expand the sub functions for the two main
functions.
Installation of monitoring unit
The allowed complexity of the installation function is rather high compared to the in-
teraction function.
The reason is that the location of the installation require cleaning
beforehand. Currently this is only possible with divers and ROVs (see section 4.4.2 on
page 48).
If we assume that the usage of divers or ROVs is impossible, we have to design the concepts
around the presence of marine growth. We deemed this to be unfeasible, since the surface
characteristics of the installation location is too unpredictable. Especially to ensure a long-
term mount of the monitoring unit. The conclusion is that the installation of monitoring
unit will require divers or ROVs for preparing the surface. Thus the added infrastructure
that divers or ROVs require can be utilized to obtain a better solution. Since cleaning
of subsea structure are already a proven technology and because we consider it unfeasible
that this concept could provide a better solution, it will not be considered further.
The installation function of the monitoring unit should be a two-step mounting procedure
that allows a temporary attachment to the surface, where adjustments in position and ori-
entation are possible. When the correct position is found a permanent mounting function
==PAGE:113==
6.2 Main functions and requirements
97
is enabled. The goal of this function is to obtain a permanent, long-term resilient interface
to the surface.
Furthermore the geometry of the monitoring unit should be designed to suit dierent
surfaces. Generally the surfaces are made of ferromagnetic steel, with varying degrees of
curvature and defects. The monitoring unit should thus allow placement on as wide a
range of dierent surface geometries as possible.
Monitoring unit interaction
The function of interacting with the monitoring unit should be as simple as possible to
enable exibility and ensure that the function does not pose constraints on the actor that
enables the interaction. In this section the assumption is made that any action that an
AUV performs, can be performed by a ROV, and in turn by a diver, but that the reasoning
does not hold true in the reverse order. Therefore if the design allows autonomy it will
inherently enable the system to be used by all three actors.
In order to design for autonomy a few parameters should be considered. The complexity of
the manipulations required should be limited to be as simple as possible. This is due to the
fact that localization precision of autonomous systems is limited. The design requirements
for the monitoring unit is thus to limit the required autonomous manipulations and to allow
a low degree of precision. Hence the interface should have an inherent form of guidance.
Additionally the unit conguration should have a low number of required manipulation
degrees of freedom and a low number of required operations to engage the interface.
Overall system
In this case the most important parameters to consider is the long term stability of the
monitoring unit, as well as the hydrodynamic prole to minimize drag and lift forces.
Furthermore the design of the monitoring unit should address the fact that marine growth
may eventually contaminate the interfaces. Interfaces should be robustly designed to cope
with all forms of degradation.
Summary
In summery, each of the discussed functions are listed along with associated sub-functions.
Each sub-function correspond to a criteria, these are the words that are emphasized. For
each criteria a number of requirements are listed.
• Installation of the monitoring unit
 Placement of unit on structure
∗Allow adjustments of position and orientation
∗Enable placement on a wide range of surface curvatures
==PAGE:114==
98
Monitoring concept
 Mounting of monitoring unit
∗Robust and reliable long term solution
∗Withstand ocean conditions for North Sea platforms
• Monitoring unit interaction
 Monitoring unit localization
∗Reliable and fault-tolerant localization method
∗Applicable with autonomous vehicles
 Data transmission of measured data to the AUV
∗Allow exibility in terms of data collection interval
 Recharging of the monitoring unit
∗Applicable with autonomous vehicles
∗Power independent during use
 End-of-life
∗Minimize waste
6.3
Generation and evaluation of monitoring concepts
In this section the early ndings and results of generating dierent monitoring concepts are
presented with the goal of achieving the functions and requirements listed in the previous
section. The means of achieving the functions are presented in section 6.3.1. Then the
form congurations of the monitoring unit is explored in section 6.3.3 on page 102.
6.3.1
Technological means of achieving functions
In g. 6.3 on the next page an overview of the identied means are listed. The means are
categorized in boxes and dierent technologies or methods are listed. This overview is the
summarized outcome from the iterative idea creation process.
Some of the means listed in g. 6.3 on the facing page were highly unlikely, discussed in
section 5.8 on page 91. It is unfeasible that the mounting of the monitoring unit can be
accomplished by welding or fasteners. This is mainly due to the risk adverse nature of the
industry. Every function that has a risk of structural damage is avoided, thus no structural
alteration of the surfaces can be made.
Within the function of data transmission sensor networks are unfeasible. Even if the data
can be transferred through a sensor network, sensors are still limited by battery power
and thus physical interaction with sensors are persistently required. Furthermore wired
data transmission are not feasible as concluded earlier. Lastly, while electrical connectors
for subsea parts do exist, it is considered unfeasible to perform subsea battery change
or charging the monitoring unit.
These operations are considered too complex for an
autonomous robot.

6.3.2
Evaluation and selection of concept
Eight dierent concepts were developed with dierent combination of the means listed in
g. 6.3. The concepts are described in appendix B.2 on page 219. The monitoring concepts
are evaluated against the requirements described in section 6.2 on page 95.
However to develop the relative weighting of the criteria, a matrix where each criteria is
scored in relation to every other one. The matrix is shown in table 6.1 on the next page.
All functions are listed in the rows, each one is then evaluated against all the columns. If
the criteria is more important than the one in the column it is scored 1. If it is not, then it
is scored 0. The diagonal, which corresponds to the evaluation of a criteria against itself,
are all scored 1. The result is a 1 through 6 evaluation of the criterias, where the most
important is scored 6 and the least important has a score of 1.
The results from table 6.1 on the following page is then used in evaluating the concepts.
The same procedure as section 5.4 on page 74 used in the system concept selection. The
results are shown in table 6.2 on the following page, the last and nal concept was identied
as the most promising concept.
In the nal concept the monitoring unit is divided into an adapter part and a sensor
part. The interface technology between the structure and the adapter is also divided into
two steps. An initial interface is established with permanent magnets, then when correct
placement is achieved an adhesive is applied. The rest of the interfaces are achieved using
an electro-permanent magnet.
These allow quick engagement and disengagements.

6.3.3
Form congurations of adapter and sensor
As many product designs, form follow the function. The main form of the adapter and
sensor conguration should simplify the functionality and easy connectivity through low
complexity guidance and avoid marine fouling issues.
To achieve this, a series of quantied structure sketches were drawn with simple variations
of the arrangements of the blocks which facilitate an expanded view of the possibilities
(Tjalve, 2003). Dierent variations are shown in appendices B.3, B.4 and B.7.
The concepts were evaluated in terms of the applicability for utilizing autonomous vehicles
to manipulate the connections.
This means that the interfaces should be designed in
such a way that low precision handling is allowed and a minimum number of operations
is required.
Furthermore the form should reect a design which strives for long term
underwater resilience and minimizes the impact of the environment, including the marine
fouling. In summary the requirements of the form are listed below.
• High autonomous vehicle applicability
 Guided engagement
 Low complexity connection
• Long-term resilience
• High environmental resistance
The process of generating these structures are described in more detail in appendices B.5
and B.14 on page 229 and on page 238).
The quantied structure that shows the greatest promise in terms of requirements is circled
out in appendix B.5. The main advantages for this concept is that the adapter has a larger
footprint than the sensor and will thus act as a barrier to marine fouling. The concept has
a hydrodynamic prole which will minimize the hydrodynamic loading on the parts. The
similar interface dimensions for both sides of the sensor, enables a similar tool interaction
when placing and removing sensors with the AUV. Adapter installation would also benet
from this generic interface. Furthermore the design enables the stacking of sensor units.
A rendered edition of the form and the stacking function is seen in g. 6.4 on the facing
page.
Stackable sensors has the advantages of enabling more compact storing under transport
and deployment. Furthermore they give the option of placing more than one sensor on the
same adapter. Building on the same interface the sensor becomes a platform for integrating
multiple sensors. The sensor-adapter solution is the tool and the platform for an array of
sensors to be build in. The interface between the sensors and the communication device
enable the design to be modular and customizable.
==PAGE:119==
6.4 The monitoring unit
103
Figure 6.4: The basic design of the monitoring unit with the advantage of a stackable
sensor
6.4
The monitoring unit
The monitoring unit is presented in its nal form in this section along with a more in-depth
discussion of the component choices. The following sections will in turn dene each aspect
of the design. An overview of the monitoring unit is presented in gs. 6.5 and 6.6 on the
next page and on page 105. The full visualisation can be seen in g. B.3 on page 240.
The monitoring unit is a simple and versatile concept for the collection of structural data.
The unit is incorporated with a strain gauge and a IMU, but the platform will allow the
operator to adapt the unit based on their requirements. With a few collar options the
adapter can be modied to dierent member diameters and with the interchangeable core
other passive sensors can be selected. The form design and components allow a uniform
guidance for the AIM system. The acoustic transmitter ensure communication to the AUV
and the NFC technology allows wireless data transfer between the adapter and sensor. The
unit is waterproof and repellent for marine foul and have a power supply that exceeds the
requested 12 months of monitoring. With the combination of the generic interface that is
stackable and the wireless NFC transmitter, multiple sensors can be synced at the same
time for a more precise calibration of the gathered data.
In g. 6.7 on page 106 an exploded view of the adapter and sensor is shown. The numbers
refer to each of the mechanical components in the bill of materials table 6.4 on page 106.
The selection of material for the collar, housing and convering is discussed in section 6.6.2
on page 128. The assembled components of the monitoring unit is shown in g. 6.7 on
page 106 and a cross section unit is shown in g. 6.9 on page 107.
6.4.1
Interface strength requirements
To estimate the maximum separation force experienced by the adapter and the sensor it
is assumed that water speeds will govern the failure modes for the system. In this section

==PAGE:124==

Monitoring concept
these speeds and the hydrodynamic forces they cause on the sensor will be estimated. As
discussed in section 4.1, The North Sea is a relatively shallow sea which means that waves
are a signicant factor of the water speed experienced by the sensors. In the chosen sensor
design there is a recess in the sensor and adapter. The recess creates a low pressure area
when uid pass on top of it. This low pressure area causes a lift force of the adapter and
the sensor. This separation force should never exceed the holding force of the adhesive
and the sensor magnet.
To estimate the maximum separation force caused by water speeds, we need to evaluate
three sources of water current.
• Wind current
• Tide current
• Wave current
All of these sources can be expanded to smaller and more complex bits. But for now
only a rough estimate of the upper bound is required. The order of magnitude of the
maximum wind-induced current speed in the North Sea is around 1 m/s (Sündermann
and Pohlmann, 2011), though it strongly depend on location. Tide currents are on the
magnitude of 0.5 m/s. Water speed caused by waves is a bit more complex.
Water speeds caused by extreme freak waves
In order to estimate how strong the interfaces should be, we investigate freak waves in
the North Sea.
The goal is to estimate the maximum extreme water speeds that the
sensor and adapter will encounter.
This estimated water speed can be plugged into a
simple Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) analysis and we can get an estimate of the
separation force, between both the adapter and sensor and between the adapter and the
structure. Linear wave theory is used to estimate the water speed. Note should be taken
that this calculation is a very rough estimate and a lot of factors is excluded. For a worst
case scenario we reference a freak wave. Freak waves are dened as a wave which is two
times signicant wave height, Hs, at that instant. Thus a freak wave is a sudden large
wave. In table 6.5 two examples of freak waves and their characteristics are shown.
Table 6.5: Freak wave characteristics (Magnusson and Donelan, 2013). Measured at the
Ekosk complex which stands at 70 meters depth

Linear wave theory has it's limitations in that most extreme wave behaviour is highly
nonlinear. However an non-linear estimation of upper bound wave speeds are out of the
scope of this report. When tide current and average current speeds are factored in, we
assume that the maximum water current speed experienced for a monitoring unit placed
in the North Sea is 10 m/s. This upper bound should never realistically be reached for the
monitoring unit.

Computational Fluid Dynamics
From the previous section, results will be sought to be converted into the upper bound
force experienced by the monitoring unit. The CAD model of the monitoring unit is rst
analyzed in the Flow Simulation section of SolidWorks by a CFD program. In table 6.7 is
the results from two CFD simulations. The plots show the relative pressure2, where red
areas are high pressure regions and blue areas are low pressure regions. As expected the
recess causes a low pressure region which causes a relatively large lift force on both the
sensor and adapter. Furthermore it seems like the forces increase in a linear way from slow
to fast speeds, however more simulations is required.

The forces on the adapter and sensor is extracted from the CFD simulation.
For the
sensor the force has two components, a separation force that pushes the sensor normally
away from the adapter and a peeling force that wedges the sensor away from the adapter.
The resultant force on the sensor gives an indication of the strength requirements of the
Electro-Permanent Magnet (EPM). This interface is expected to a critical interface, as the
only component provinding force is the EPM.
For the adapter the drag and lift components of the maximum force corresponds to the
tensile and shear strength of the adhesive. The row with slow water speeds corresponds
to average conditions and these numbers give an indication of the required strength of the
temporary permanent magnets used in the adapter. The results are listed in table 6.8 on
the next page.
The conclusion of this analysis is that the interface strengths should at least achieve the
forces listed in table 6.9 on the facing page. The CAD model is an early stage version with
a lower prole, but sharper corners. Forces may be increased, but the new CAD have a
more curved prole to accommodate this issue.

6.4.2
Electronics
In this section the goal is to estimate the size and specications of the required electri-
cal components. The subsections will in detail describe the components respectivly. An
overview of the proposed required electrical components are illustrated in g. 6.10 on the
next page.
In essence the sensor is an active battery-powered unit.
The sensor can communicate
with the AUV through an acoustic modem. The modem is in a low-power listening mode,
when it receives a wake-up call from the AUV the modem starts to provide localization
information to the AUV. When the AUV approaches, the sensor can use near eld EM
communication through the NFC circuit to provide a two-way communication link to the
AUV. The AUV can utilize this communication link to request data from the unit or
disable the force connection between the sensor's EPM and the steel core of the adapter
and thus release the sensor from the adapter.
The sensor consists of a battery and a IMU that measures motion data which is then
processed by the microprocessor and stored in the storage module.
The adapter part is a passive, permanently mounted unit. The adapter core hold the
interchangeable measuring unit, see g. 6.11 on the following page, which in this concept
is a strain gauge. The strain gauge is connected to a signal conditioning circuit which pre-
pares the signal for wireless transfer. Power is transferred wirelessly through an inductive
coupling between the sensor and adapter enabled by the NFC system.

6.4 The monitoring unit
113
Measurement
An Inertial Measurement Unit and a strain gauge are the two main measurement units
used in the design. In terms of mounting the two technologies have dierent requirements.
Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU)
An Inertial Measurement Unit (IMU) basically consists of three accelerometers and three
gyroscopes. When the information is merged a complete picture of the movement of the
IMU can be recovered. In this case the interest lies in the movement of the structure where
the sensor is mounted. This means that the mounting of the IMU inuences the quality
of the data output greatly. If correct data output is to be ensured the mounting needs
to have a natural frequency greater than the maximum driving frequency of interest. See
section 6.4.2 on the next page for more information. However the IMU does not require a
direct surface contact, which allows it to be placed in the active and replaceable component.
In table 6.10 a selection of low power IMUs are shown along with their dimensions.
Table 6.10: The specications of commercially available Inertial Measurement Units
(IMU).
Manufacturer
Model
Power
Bias Stability

a (MAX21100 2014)

Accelerometer
An accelerometer is a sensor capable of measuring inertial acceleration. The acceleration
is related, through Newton's second law of motion, to the force acting on the input axis
direction. As also mentioned before, to provide a three-dimensional solution, 3 accelerom-
eters are needed, one for each orthogonal axis. The 3 accelerometers mounts together in an
almost orthogonal cluster (mainly due to imperfections), also known as triad. The perfect
orthogonal reference frame dened by the accelerometers ideal orthogonal input directions
is dened as the platform reference frame. However since the accelerometers reference
frames relation to the global frame is unknown (due to the fact that no constrain is placed
on the placement and orientation of the sensors) then a set of gyroscopes are needed as
well (Vectornav, 2014).
==PAGE:130==
114
Monitoring concept
Gyroscope
A gyroscope is a type of sensor that measures angular rate. When calibrated a gyroscope
provides information about the change of orientation with respect to the calibrated refer-
ence frame. Thus the combination of gyroscopes and accelerometers allows the full motion
picture of the member of interest to be reconstructed.
The goal of this system is to operate for an extended period, up to a year. A such a duration
the drift of the gyroscope turns out to be an important parameter for IMU selection. In
manufacturers sheets this parameter is called bias stability. Further information about the
error stability of gyroscopes can be found in (Weinberg, 2011).
Transmissibility of vibration energy
Vibration energy needs to be transmitted through the adapter and onto the sensor which
contains the accelerometer. The amount of transmitted energy is governed by the trans-
missiblility of the system. In general the system should be designed such that the lowest
natural frequency of the mount should greatly exceed the highest driving natural frequency
(i.e. the frequency to be measured). In this case this requirement is not very strict as the
driving frequency is very low (Inman, 2007).
Wireless strain gauge circuit
In (Yao et al., 2015) the authors describe a method of wirelessly interrogating a strain
gauge using low power. The setup can measure both static and dynamic strain however
the setup is powered by a solar energy harvester. The measured power consumption of the
strain gauge circuit is 10.6 mW. In (Swedberg, 2015) the author describes a method of
monitoring rebar reinforcements in concrete tunnels. The authors places an assembly of a
pre-mounting strain gauge on a rebar which is placed inside the concrete. This strain gauge
can then be interrogated using passive RFID technology. The important conclusion is that
it is possible to construct an electrical circuit where the sensing part does not include a
power source, but the power transferred wirelessly is modulated and reected back to the
interrogator. The datasheet for this technology can be found in (Passive RFID Wireless
Strain Gauge 2015).
The merging of the two ideas would be of great value. A passive strain gauge circuit that
can measure dynamic strains and the ability to reect these measurements back to the
interrogating sensor unit. The power consumption given in (Yao et al., 2015) gives an
indication of the order of magnitude of the wireless power transfer requirement.
In (McGinnis, Henze, and Conroy, 2007) the authors has created an inductive coupling to
wireless transfer power to an AUV. In the article the authors achieve an eciency of 70 %
for a lab experiment, however the power transferred is much larger than the requirements
for this system. Since the eciency might be much lower for small power transfers it is
assumed that the real eciency will be 10 %.
==PAGE:131==
6.4 The monitoring unit
115
Strain gauge electrical circuit
The basic electronic circuit of a strain gauge measurement setup is shown in g. 6.12. In
practice many dierent electronic components are added to the circuit depending on the
application. The circuit consists of the strain gauge which sits in a completion bridge.
The most common type of loop is a whetstone bridge. When the strain gauge is subjected
to strain, the resistance of the gauge changes and the bridge becomes unbalanced. The
bridge is connected to a power source and a signal conditioning circuit (in the basic sketch
this circuit is a simple gain).
Figure 6.12: The essential components of a strain gauge measurement circuit (Homann,
2012)
Storage requirements
In order to estimate storage requirements of the sensor the data sampling rate needs to be
estimated. In vibration analysis Shannon's sampling theorem states that the sampling rate
should be around 2.5 times higher than the highest frequency of interest (Inman, 2007).
In this case 5 Hz is the maximum global frequency of interest3 and thus an initial sampling
rate of 13 Hz should suce for reconstructing the acceleration signal. This sampling rate
is rather low for most modern equipment, so it should be investigated if power savings can
be achieved by switching to sleep mode between samples.
However according to an interview with Ulf Tyge Tygesen from Rambøll, see appendix A.10
on page 194. The sampling theorem only provides enough data points for reconstructing
3Estimation from the interview in appendix A.10 on page 194
==PAGE:132==
116
Monitoring concept
the acceleration signal, since the displacements are also interesting, the sampling frequency
should be 10 to 20 times higher. Furthermore in some cases higher frequency monitoring is
required locally, increasing the sampling frequency required. We assume that the highest
sampling frequency that would be required is 200 Hz. This should allow a sucient amount
of data for analysis.
The amount of bytes required to store the variables depend on the accuracy chosen. For
this estimate 9 integers were assumed as sucient to store the data points. The amount of
bytes were then calculated using the following formula, where n is the amount of integers
chosen. The result is rounded up to the nearest integer.

6.5.1
Structure-Adapter Interface
Within the structure-adapter interface there are four functional units each contributing to
the interface, the adhesive, the adapter collar, the permanent magnet and the membrane.
Each one has several functions to serve.
• Adhesive (see section 6.5.1)
 Provide long-lasting bond to structure surface
 Easy to apply for a ROV
• Adapter collar
 Provide a large surface area for adhesive
• Permanent magnet (see section 6.5.1 on page 122)
 Temporary interface engagement for adjustments
 Backing force to adhesive while curing
• Membrane (see section 6.5.1 on page 124)
 Ensure adhesive ow between strain gauge and wall
 Backing force for strain gauge while adhesive curing
Underwater adhesive
Underwater adhesion has traditionally been dicult to achieve, however research revealed
signicant progress in this eld of study. According to sources underwater adhesion is plau-
==PAGE:136==
120
Monitoring concept
sible, but further adhesive selection and testing is necessary to determined the feasibility
(Bonis and Scala, 2007; Bonis et al., 2013). The material choice has a signicant impact
on the strength of the bond. Materials should have high surface energy and the surfaces
of the materials should be prepared as to have an optimal surface roughness (Packham,
2003).
Figure 6.14: Adhesive is forced in between the cavities of the adapter and the member
by the ROV
As shown g. 6.14 the adhesive is applied by the workclass robot and injected through
the adapter-robot interface. With the applied pressure the adhesive will spread out from
the middle and remove the water between the member and the adapter. In this process
the strain gauge should be covered with glue and bonded to the member described in
section 6.5.1 on page 124
Surface bonding
Generally bonding two solid surfaces underwater is a challenge. The reason is that the
adhesive uid has to displace the water molecules that sticks to the solid surfaces. Air is a
much easier uid to displace than water is. The stickiness of uid is a complex interaction
between several mechanisms. According to (Awaja et al., 2009) no single value will describe
how well two surfaces will adhere with a given adhesive. However the surface energy of the
two surfaces to be joined can be used as a general rule of thumb, for more information refer
to (Packham, 2003). A high surface energy generally improves adhesion and a low surface
energy decreases adhesion, g. 6.15 on the next page The surface energies of some common
materials are found in (Converting 3M Technology into Successful Applications 2015). The
surface energy of certain polymers can be increased by specic surface treatments, e.g. heat
treatment (Farris et al., 2010).
In (Bonis and Scala, 2007; Bonis et al., 2013) the Army Research Laboratory test the
underwater curing properties of dierent commercially available adhesives. Additives and
modiers were developed to create adhesives that quickly formed strong bonds between
water saturated surfaces and cure suciently in the presence of water. The tensile and
shear strength of the underwater applied and cured adhesives were tested. In table 6.16
on the facing page the requirements for the adhesive are presented.
==PAGE:137==
6.5 Interface solutions
121
Figure 6.15: Dierence in surface energy
Table 6.16: Adhesive requirements
Curing requirements
Can be applied and cure in harsh North Sea environment
• Can cure at low temperatures
• Can cure in salt water
• Curing time should be below 24 hours
• Low viscosity uid to ensure that it will ow properly in channels.
Bond strength requirements
Ensures a permanent strong bond between structure and adapter.
• Adhesion force must keep the adapter in place for the duration of the life-time.
Strain gauge backing requirements
Ensures proper bonding of the strain gauge to the surface with the following
requirements (Figliola and Beasley, 1995, see p. 493)
• Adhesive strength should be sucient to transmit the expected strain
• The thermal conduction and expansion of the adhesive should be suitable for the
application.
• Adhesive curing expansion and shrinkage should be minimized as this creates
apparent strain in the strain gauge
Adhesive selection
Adhesion resins can be made in both single part or two part systems and many are designed
to specic purposes. Epoxy is a two-part, thermoset plastic mix, consisting of an epoxy
resin and hardener or a curing agent. Generally epoxy glue have a property of creating ex-
cellent adhesion due to the strong polar bonds it forms with the surface it comes in contact
with and because of the curring agent, the resin is stable until mixed. The applications
and variations of epoxy are many and with the right curing agent, it includes underwater
adhesion. Both single and two part adhesion were investigated in this research.
In table 6.17 on the next page a list of commercially available two-component epoxies is
shown. All of these epoxies can be applied and cured underwater. Only one of them has
data on the expected tensile strength though.

Permanent Magnet
As noted in table 6.17, adhesives usually have rather long curing times. Therefore, to avoid
having the robot provide the temporary pressure required, a simple permanent magnet is
added instead. The magnet will perform the following functions
==PAGE:139==
6.5 Interface solutions
123
• Establishing a temporary bond to the surface allowing the following subfunctions
 Enabling readjustments in the placement and orientation of the adapter allowing
a more precise placement
 Ensure that the adapter stays in place while the adhesive cures
Some of the strongest commercially available magnets are made of neodymnium. Neodym-
nium comes in dierent grades that are categorized from N32 to N52. A higher number
indicates a better quality, stronger and more expensive magnet. These magnets come in
several dierent form factors, but cannot be machined without special training and tools
(the material are brittle and the dust produced are highly ammable). For the purpose of
presentation a ring magnet were chosen with the specications shown in table 6.19.

Placing a at object on curving surfaces
The surface which the adapter is mounted on will almost always be a curving surface.
The problem is, how large can the adapter be while still t reasonably on most member
diameters. While the distribution of member diameters are unknown, the approximate
range is between 600 to 6000 mm. For the purpose of this estimation a normal distribution
is assumed to adequately describe the real variation in member diameters.
The rst criteria can be set as the contact area of the adapter cannot sit more than 5 mm
from the member. The common member diameter is assumed to be 3000 mm. This yields
the following maximum sensor diameter
a = 2
p
h(D −h) = 245 mm
(6.5)
Thus if the adapter have a maximum diameter of 245 mm it will have no more than 5 mm
on more than 50 % of the members used in the jacket structures in the North Sea.
Although, the assumption of the distribution have not been conrmed, the at adapter
would only match members with a diameter of 3000 mm or greater. This leaves half of the
members unsuited for mounting of the at adapter. To mitigate this our design includes
a range of three collars which accommodates the varying member dimension, see g. 6.16
on the next page.
Another issue is the problem of mounting the permanent magnets on curving surfaces.
The attraction force of magnets rapidly decrease with the distance to the power of four, it
is therefore essential to design such that magnet placement will have the shortest possible
distance to the steel wall. A minimum mountable member diameter can be estimated if
==PAGE:140==
124
Monitoring concept
Figure 6.16: Collar selection. Depending on the pipe diameter, one of three collar sizes
is selected.
a maximum allowable gap between the outer perimeter of the magnet and the surface is
set. If a Ø50 mm magnet with a maximum allowable air gap of 1 mm is assumed, then
these parameters can be plugged into equation (6.5). The result is the minimum required
member diameter of 625 mm. This member diameter should allow the adapter a good
magnetic contact with the member surface. These numbers correspond fairly well with the
dimensions of the magnet and for the minimum member diameter.
Membrane unit
The membrane unit consists of a membrane and a strain gauge. The membrane's main
function is critical to the functionality of the strain gauge. The strain gauge is mounted on
the center of the membrane. The resting position of the membrane allows the strain gauge
to protrude from the mounting plane of the adapter. This ensures that the strain gauge
will always be in contact with the member surface. The main functions for the membrane
are as follows
• Allow adhesive to ow between the strain gauge and the member surface
• Apply local pressure to the strain gauge to ensure the smallest gap between the strain
gauge and the surface of the member
The main function of the strain gauge is as follows
• Accurately measure the strain of the structure
• Orientation-independent of principal strain directions, as these may not be known
prior to mounting
Membrane functionality
The membrane performs two main functions, rstly it acts as a simple spring providing
backing force to the strain gauge and secondly as a barrier between two pressure chambers.
==PAGE:141==
6.5 Interface solutions
125
The membrane function is shown in gs. 6.17 and 6.18. When adhesive is applied, it ows
through a channel and pressurizes the lower chamber.
Since another channel ensures
ambient pressure in the upper chamber a pressure dierential between the compartments
will occur, causing the membrane to compress and move away from the member. This
allows the adhesive to ow between the strain gauge and the member, see g. 6.17. When
the lower chamber is lled the adhesive, pressure forces the ow in the member-adapter
interface. When sucient adhesive has been injected the injection pressure is removed and
the pressure dierential between the two compartments will equalize. This removes the
compressing force on the membrane allowing the spring eect of the membrane to apply a
backing force to the strain gauge, ensuring a proper bond, see g. 6.18.
Figure 6.17: Adhesive is injected into the lower chamber, causing the membrane to com-
press
Figure 6.18: Injection pressure is removed and the membrane applies a backing force to
the strain gauge
6.5.2
Adapter-Sensor Interface
This interface serves as the communication link between the adapter and the sensor. It is
also one of the interfaces which needs to allow manipulation of an autonomous robot. This
interface consists of 3 functional units; the electro-permanent magnet, the NFC antenna
and the wetted surfaces.
The main functions of the electro-permanent magnet are as follows
• Simple engagement and disengagement
 Function needs to be switched on and o by an autonomous drone
• Holding force is above environmental loads on a medium time scale.
• Must transmit vibration energy from adapter to sensor accurately.
==PAGE:142==
126
Monitoring concept
The main function of the two NFC antennas are as follows
• Allow wireless power transfer
• Allow data readings from the adapter to the sensor
The main function of the wetted surfaces is as follows
• Surfaces are resistant to marine fouling
6.5.3
Sensor-Robot Interface
This interface consists of the following functional units, an acoustic communication link,
an electromagnetic communication link and a electro-permanent magnet
In order to reap the benets of stackable sensors the decision of making the sensor side of
the interface equal to the adapter side of the adapter-sensor interface.
The main function of the acoustic communication link
• Provide localization information to the autonomous robot
• Provide localization information for lost sensors
• Conrming sensor location
The main function of the electromagnetic communication link
• Allow the robot to communicate with the sensor
 Allow robot to control the adapter-sensor interface
The main function of the electro-permanent magnet
• Allow the robot to attach to the sensor
6.5.4
Adapter-Robot Interface
The active components in this interface is the electro-permanent magnet and the adhesive
injector .
The adapter-robot interface has two main functions to perform.
• Facilitate adhesive injection from robot to adapter
• Maintain adapter-robot connection during adhesive injection
• Disengage the interface after injection
The requirements for this interface have been identied as
• Allow adhesive to ow from the robot to the adapter.
==PAGE:143==
6.6 Material selection
127
• Enable interface disengagement with the electro-permanent magnet
• Minimize risk of adhesive owing in unwanted locations
The installation of the monitoring unit is performed by a workclass ROV. This function
is designed as to only be performed once for every adapter. However the detailing of how
the functions are performed on the robot side of this interface is out of the scope of this
project.
The interface is sketched in g. 6.19. The robot injects the adhesive through a channel as
illustrated in g. 6.14 on page 120. By moving the injection channel to the adapter collar,
the risk of contaminating the adapter-sensor interface is reduced.

injection
6.6
Material selection
In this section the considerations regarding the selection of materials for the dierent
parts are discussed. Only the parts which are not bought o the shelf are considered. An
overview of the parts are shown in table 6.4 on page 106. The dierent parts are categorized
in non-wetted and wetted parts. The following list shows which parts are considered in
this section.
• Non-wetted parts
 Core
 Disk
 Membrane
• Wetted parts
 Collar
 Housing
==PAGE:144==
128
Monitoring concept
 Covering
6.6.1
Non-wetted parts
Non wetted surfaces are the parts that are not in direct contact with water.
The main function of the core is the ability to transmit vibration energy to the IMU in
the sensor, as discussed in section 6.4.2 on page 114. Another function of the core is that
EPM of the sensor mounts on the surface of the core. As such the adapter core should be
made in a sti and magnetic material. Since it is rotational symmetric, we suggest that
turned high-strength steel is used. However since the core will have some surfaces that are
in contact with water we suggest that it is also coated (see section 4.2.7) to decrease the
corrosion process.
The function of the disk is to allow magnetic connections to the sensor and such it should
be made of a magnetic material. We suggest a high-strength steel that is coated after cut
from a rod.
The functionality of the membrane is discussed in section 6.5.1. It is important to the
overall functionality of the adhesion process that the membrane retracts when adhesive
pressure is applied and provides enough backing pressure for the strain gauge when adhesive
pressure is relieved. As such the stiness of the membrane should be chosen carefully. The
adhesive pressure should be taken into consideration, as well as the strength of the ring
magnet and the backing pressure required by strain gauge mounting. We suggest that the
membrane is made of an exible polymer material such as silicone.
6.6.2
Wetted parts
Both the adapter and the sensor has surfaces that are in contact with water.
These
surfaces provide protection for the internal components in the monitoring unit and such
the materials selected are critical to ensure the proper functioning of the monitoring system.
The requirements are list and prioritized below. These requirements are translated into
the material parameters and the list can be viewed as an initial starting point.
1. Have excellent bonding properties with the adhesive
(a) High Surface energy to assist adhesion, for surfaces in contact with adhesive
2. Waterproof
(a) Low water transmissibility
3. Good chemical resistance
(a) Salt water resistance
(b) Oil and production uid resistances
4. Long-lasting
(a) High stiness
(b) High impact resistance
==PAGE:145==
6.6 Material selection
129
(c) High fracture toughness
5. Resistant towards marine fouling
(a) Low surface energy to resist marine fouling, for surfaces not in contact with
adhesive, see (Callow and Fletcher, 1994).
6. Low material and production cost
(a) Low material cost per kg
(b) Low machining and/or molding energy
Initially there might seem to be a conict between requiring a high surface energy for
maximum adhesion and a low surface energy for marine fouling resistance. However as
discussed in section 6.5.1 on page 119 a surface treatment called ame-treatment can be
applied to reduce the surface energy of certain polymers. We assume that ame-treatment
does indeed allow the adhesion of low surface energy polymers and this makes them the
preferred choice as to allow both high and low surface energy on the surfaces that have
specic requirements.
Three dierent polymers are listed in table 6.20. All three polymer types are good choices.
PVC is recommended for use with the adhesives due to a relatively high surface energy and
PVC also has a very high impact strength however it is mediocre in elasticity modulus,
hardness and cost. PE-HD on the other hand is average in most categories but excels
in being cheap.
PP is superior in most categories but is also the most expensive.
In
conclusion we expect that PE-HD will perform well enough and it is chosen for both the
adapter collar, the sensor housing and the sensor covering. The housing and covering both
has a geometry with approximately equal wall size and thus they should both be able to
be injection molded. The collar on the other hand, varies greatly in wall thickness and
should either be molded or turned from a rod.

6.7.1
Strain gauge selection
In this section some of the parameters that are important when selecting a strain gauge is
discussed. Many dierent parameters determine the success of strain gauge measurements.
Strain gauges come in a wide selection of geometries, depending on the application. Ac-
cording to (Homann, 2012, p. 35) the parameters listed in table 6.21 on the next page
provides a good starting point.
We suggest using a triaxial rosette arranged in a 60◦pattern. Using a triaxial strain gauges
allows the adapter to be placed at any orientation while the principal stress directions
and magnitudes can be recovered afterwards. The arrangement of the gauge allows the
measurement point to be located in the center of the membrane.

6.7.2
Electro-permanent Magnets
Electro-Permanent Magnet (EPM) are similar to electro magnets in the way that applying
power switches the state of the magnet. While electro magnets are naturally in an o
state, EPMs are naturally in an on state. Two types of electro-permanent magnets exist,
one that requires sustained power to demagnetize the EPM. This type is, in this thesis,
referred to as active demagnetized. The other type requires energy to switch between the
on and o state, these are, in this thesis, referred to as switchable state. This allows the
EPM to apply a force while consuming no power. EPMs require energy proportional to
their volume and will exert force proportional to their area, these factors favor low energy
operations at small dimensions.
At the centimeter scale, EPMs can hold weight hundreds of times heavier than their own.
They exert force comparable to their weight at distances comparable to their own length.
==PAGE:148==
132
Monitoring concept
EPMs operates at lower voltages and allow a larger air gap than electro magnets, however
they have a higher prole (Knaian, 2010).
For two cylindrical magnets the force between them can be approximated by the following
equation when the air gap is far larger than the height of the magnets.
F(x) = 3πµ0
2
M2R4h2 1
x4
(6.6)
Where R is the radius of the magnets, h is the height of the magnets and x is the air gap
between the magnets. We can conclude that the force decreases rapidly with air gap and
great care should be taken to ensure that the magnets have a close contact as possible.
Other design parameters are obviously that the magnets should have a surface area, R, as
large as possible and should, to a lesser degree, be as tall as possible, for maximum holding
force and allowable air gap.
Some commercially available EPMs are shown in table 6.22. The dimensions and forces
attainable by these EPMs are in good agreement with the dimension and power consump-
tion that can be expected to be feasible. However since the holding force drops sharply
with separation distance, this part of the concept could be investigated more thoroughly
in future work, for example by testing the holding force with increased separation.

Chapter 7
Function proof-of-concept testing
In this chapter the procedure of testing some of the core functionalities of the monitoring
concept is described.
We have two main hypothesise to test
1. During applied injection the membrane will deect caused by the increase pressure.
2. Injecting epoxy adhesive underwater can create a sucient bonding force between
two surfaces.
a) The bounding is strong enough to support the concept.
b) Applying open cell foam will guide the ow and increase coverage.
7.1
Membrane proof-of-concept test
The functionality of the membrane as described in section 6.5.1 on page 124 are sought
to be tested. Specically it was sought to be conrmed that with sucient sealing of the
membrane chamber and a uid pressure applied below the membrane, the membrane would
disengaging the surface and return when pressure were released. As a proof-of-concept a
simple test setup were build.
Experimental setup
Sheets of acrylic were laser cut and bonded together to form a Ø 80mm geometry that
resembles the concept core geometry. The membrane component were sourced from a Ø
==PAGE:150==
134
Function proof-of-concept testing
40mm small suction cup and glued onto the acrylic. With a 60ml syringe, colored water
were injected manually through a 3 mm. hole into the membrane chamber. Along the
ring of the acrylic geometry a 1mm foam ring were applied. The chamber were then sealed
with a Ø 80 mm acrylic disc and the setup held together with 4 small clamps.
(a) Liquid applied with syringe
(b) Liquid lling the chamber
(c) Membrane deect. Liquid forced out
(d) Side view
Figure 7.1: The membrane test setup. The colored water is injected into the membrane
chamber. Once the chamber is lled, the pressure raises lifting the membrane
and forcing the liquid out
Results
During the injection of the uid the membrane stayed at rest. Only when the applied
pressure exceeded the spring tension in the chamber the membrane lifted and once the
pressure on the syringe were released, the membrane return to its resting position. With
a test of a 1:1 scale setup the hypothesise were proven for the giving liquid.
We saw
a signicant deection in the test membrane as the liquid were forced in. It should be
noted that epoxy adhesive would ow slower and further testing should be done to conrm
similar behavior. It will be excepted a greater resistance once the chamber is lled due to
the higher viscosity of the epoxy.
==PAGE:151==
7.2 Adhesive strength test
135
7.2
Adhesive strength test
In section 6.5.1 on page 119 the challenges of adhesively bonding surfaces underwater were
described. While sources indicate that the concept is possible, a quick proof-of-concept
test were decided to be valuable. Essentially the concept consists of the adapter and a steel
surface that is joined adhesively. In this test the adapter is substituted for a test specimen.
The rst goal of the test is conrming the possibility of adhesively joining surfaces while
under water. The second goal is to estimate the adhesive force of these surfaces. These
goals can be stated as questions as follows
• Is the adhesive force greater than zero [N]?
• How large is the adhesive strength [N/m2]?
The factors that are thought to aect the ability to adhere and the strength of the resulting
bond are listed in table 7.1 on the next page. While a study into the eects of all these
factors would be nice, it was decided that the focus should lie on the following parameters
• Adhesive brand
• Material type of test specimen
The experiment should be designed such that the inuence of the other eects are min-
imized.
In order to achieve this all experiments should be done at the same time, on
the same test surface, submerged in the same water, experience the same curing time, be
conducted with approximately the same adhesive injection pressure and the same back-
ing pressure. The test specimens should have the similar surface roughness and the same
geometry.
Two types of adhesives were sourced, a two-component epoxy from Loctite and a putty
type from JP weld. It is already given that the adapter will be adhesively joined to a steel
surface, so by having a large plate which ts all of the experiments we can eliminate the
roughness and cleanness of the test surface as inuencing factors, as these have the same
impact on all of the experiments. Furthermore it is also assumed that varying steel grades
does not signicantly inuence results. By allowing a suciently curing time it is possible
to measure the maximum obtainable strength.
In summary the factors tested are the material type of the specimen, a quantitative factor
which is tested at 3 levels, and the adhesive brand, a qualitative factor which is tested at
two levels. The level designation is shown in table 7.2 on the following page.
A standard 23 full factorial design is proposed (Montgomery, 2008).
This experiment
design will allow a statistical analysis of whether there is a statistical signicant variance
between surface types and adhesive brands. As noted in the experimental design run 3
and 5 are replicates as are run 4 and 6, this allows the error variance to be calculated.
Furthermore the quadratic eect of the material type are estimated.

Function proof-of-concept testing

7.2.1
Test preparation
The geometry of the test specimen was chosen to resemble the adapter as closely as possible.
However, to reduce test complexity and resources a scale model was decided upon. The
discs were produced to be around 26 mm in diameter with a small threaded hole in the
middle. The threaded hole allows a bolt to be mounted, after the adhesive is applied.
When the adhesive has cured then the bolt enables the application of force to test the
tensile strength of the bond.
To maximise the tensile strength of the epoxy bond, the surface roughness needs to fall in
the range from 3 to 6 Ra (Kunio Uehara, 2002). The surface roughness was measured using

is not possible to statistically say if roughness has an eect as it is confounded with the
experimental error. However it is expected that roughness has an eect on tensile strength
of the bond (Kunio Uehara, 2002). The diameter of the eight discs were measured were
measured using a caliper.

7.3
Injected liquid ow pattern test
In order to ensure that water would be displaced from the interface between the structure
and the adapter, the adhesive will be applied from the membrane chamber in the center
of the adapter. The objective of the test was to investigate the ow pattern of an injected
liquid.
The uid was injected into a channel in a full scale prototype of our concept.
The channel has similar geometry to the ow channel in which adhesive is injected in the
monitoring unit concept, see g. 7.9 on the next page.
Between the contact surfaces 3 mm open-cell polyurethane foam were added as to ensure
that the chamber with the membrane would allow a larger relative pressure to be built up.
The foam also allows the uid to ow evenly towards the periphery of the prototype.
Experimental setup
A set of full-scale scale acrylic plates were laser cut to the dimension of the monitoring
unit. These plates were joined using acrylic cement. The acrylic plates are transparent so
it allowed us to see the ow pattern when liquid was injected.
==PAGE:160==
144
Function proof-of-concept testing
Results
The results are shown in g. 7.9. In all cases the liquid ows out approximately evenly in
all directions. However for the dry test without foam it is very dicult to see (g. 7.9a).
The dry test with foam shows a more clear ow pattern with almost equal directions,
however we see a slight tendency that the ow seeks to ow faster opposite the injection
side. This fact might be an indication that optimisation of channel geometry is required.
However this is out of the scope of this thesis.

Chapter 8
Conclusion
In this thesis we have identied a clear need for monitoring the structural stress and move-
ments of oshore oil and gas platforms. In this set of data lies valuable information of the
specic damage caused by dierent loading scenarios. The collection of damage history
information improves decision-making in terms of advanced inspections and maintenance
of structures, which in the end reduces operational costs. Furthermore the historic damage
information can be used to rene damage process predictions for new optimized platform
designs. Monitoring and simple inspections lays the foundation for establishing this knowl-
edge base. Monitoring and inspection of platforms are essentially two tasks of the same
objective, which is to reduce the risk of platform failure.
Undoubtedly autonomous underwater vehicles will play a signicant role in the future in the
oil and gas industry as navigation and communication technology matures. The potential
in added eciency and safety benets by enabling autonomous inspections simply cannot
be underestimated. However while inspections provide invaluable information about the
current state of the platform it is still one-sided. Implementing monitoring systems allows
rigorous decision-making for the inspection strategy.
Therefore we conclude that a serious investment from the oil and gas industry in the
development of large scale autonomous underwater inspection vehicles can and will provide
the necessary condence in the continuous operation of oshore structures.
The system design proposed in this thesis has the potential to signicantly reduce the com-
plexity of simple inspections and monitoring tasks, by outlining and designing a framework
for a completely autonomous inspection and monitoring system. The current implementa-
tions of autonomous inspections and monitoring technologies have signicant limitations
in scope and scale, but the combination allow these limitations to be mitigated.
The proposed system oers exibility in nearly every aspect. The monitoring technologies
are not limited to strain and motion measurements, but can be adapted to any kind of
technology that ts the spacial and power specications.
The monitoring unit can be
==PAGE:162==
146
Conclusion
modied to dierent member dimensions and sensors can be coupled for longer operation
if needed. The scalability of the system allows the exact amount of measurement points for
any given application. The adaptability of the system allows easy conversion to oshore
wind farms or other man-made subsea structures. The system does not inherently pose
any constraints on the type of vehicle used to maintain the system. Data collection and
inspection could be achieved by a ROV or diver and in the future a wireless sensor network
could partially replace some functions of the autonomous robot.
This system is the rst step on the road to a complete framework of continuous data
collection, enabling real-time assessment and instantaneous damage detection for otherwise
high-cost and dicult-to-inspect underwater structures. We have hereby provided a low-
complexity, scalable system platform that is mechanically feasible and designed for full
autonomous operation.
==PAGE:163==
Chapter 9
Further Work
From this project many more interesting challenges emerged than what we have chosen to
cover in this thesis. It is our hope that this thesis could inspire and lead to further study
by building on the presented work and results.
Electrical proof-of-concept
Design an electrical circuit with a passive NFC antenna and a strain gauge with high data
reliability and signal-to-noise ratio. A test should conclude whether dierent materials,
saltwater or a changed sensor signicantly impacts the data transmission.
Strain gauge measurement accuracy
An study into the expected highest achievable accuracy in strain measurement would be
very valuable for the further development of this system. The study could involve the
preparation of the member surface, underwater mounting of the strain gauge and the
comparison of measurements with a predetermined strain.
Sensor replacement proof-of-concept
An interesting direction of work would be to mock-up the function of sensor replacement.
We suggest that an EPM is mounted on a ROV, which then attempts to dismount a sensor
that is attached to a pre-mounted adapter. The goal would be to identify hidden design
aws.
==PAGE:164==
148
Further Work
Adhesive injection
A practical test of injecting adhesive through a prototyped adapter and concluding if
the mechanics comply with this thesis' hypotheses and tests.
Multiple tests could be
performed on simulated oshore members to investigate the bonding force in relation to
coating, saltwater, marine fouling and the level of surface cleanliness.
Adhesive ow
Run simulations to optimally design the adhesive ow between the adapter and a steel
member with varying diameters. Direction, orientation and sudden disengagement of the
adapter should be considered and what eects it has on the bonding force.
Clock synchronization
The sensors could experience time delays causing them to go out of sync over a period of
time thereby complicating data analysis. It should be investigated whether the time loss
is signicant and could have an impact. An interesting topic could be using NFC when
sensors are stacked to synchronize clocks or investigating the feasibility of synchronizing
clocks when the AUV interacts with the sensors.
Optimal Sensor placement
If only a small set of monitoring units can be placed on the structure then the problem
of placing them optimally becomes very interesting. An algorithm that places the sensors
optimally based on the number available could be developed. The topic of sensor placement
on a truss structure for optimal damage detection is discussed in (Cobb and Liebst, 1997).
Enabling real-time data collection
Identifying the specic technical challenges for implementing a real-time wireless data
collection system is another interesting topic. This could for example be achieved through
an acoustic or EM sensor network, see for example (May et al., 2009, page 3).
Data redundancy
Approached could be explored of how to reduce measurements errors by integrating data
from redundant sources. When increasing the number of sensors or adding multiple inde-
pendent monitoring systems the data redundancy would increase. By correlating structural
==PAGE:165==
149
strain with acoustic scans, laser measurements or GPS tracking a more robust system could
improve the data quality by reducing the error variance.
