Glossary
caisson is a watertight retaining structure. These are constructed such that the water
can be pumped out. A caisson is used for temporary storage of oil. 38
design life is the structure's expected lifetime set at the time of structure design. Design
life is some times referred to as life expectancy in theory. 16
eld is a large area which contains oil and gas reservoirs. 13
xed platform is a type of oshore platform used for the production of oil or gas. These
platforms are built on concrete and/or steel legs anchored directly onto the seabed.
2
jackup is a type of mobile platform that consists of a buoyant hull tted with a number
of movable legs, capable of raising its hull over the surface of the sea. 2
life extension refers to continued operation of an installation beyond the expected design
life at the design phase. 16
processing platform is the type of oshore platform the processes the chemical mixture
that is extracted from wells. 12
riser is a pipe that connects an oshore structure to a sub-sea system either for drilling,
production, injection and export. 20, 22, 38
service life is the structure's expected lifetime in terms of serviceability. Service life is
some times referred to as life expectancy in practice. Service life diers from design
life for example if the surrounding conditions of the structure changes. 16
splash zone is the area immediately above and below the mean water level. It is the area
which transitions between air and water. 30
structural integrity is the ability of an structure to hold together under a load, including
its own weight, resisting breakage or bending. 16
topside is the part of a platform that is above the waterline. 48
==PAGE:170==
154
Datasheets
weld toe or a llet weld is the process of joining two pieces of metal together whether
they be perpendicular or at an angle. 49
well is the access point from the seabed to the oil and gas reservoirs below. 12, 13
XT is short for Christmas tree. A XT is a vertical assembly of mechanical components
used in oil exploration and production. It is primarily used for ow control. The
shape of a XT roughly resembles a Christmas tree. 12

MEMO 
To 
Whom it May Concern 
From Claus Myllerup 
Scientific Director, Facilities and Materials 
26 January 2016
 
Structural Integrity and Reliability Discipline 
 
Executive Summary 
The DUC production platforms are ageing and the Scientific Framework for the Danish Hydrocarbon 
Research and Technology Centre (DHRTC) therefore highlights the need to develop cost effective 
means to extend life of the offshore structures without compromising safety and reliability. 
Furthermore, in recent years the MOG DBU has gathered field data suggesting that the design 
assumptions relating to Structural Integrity and Reliability (SIR) risks to be revisited with respect to 
extreme waves. This work will use state-of-the-art to re-assess SIR risk, with priority given to the 
manned facilities. 
 
The unmanned Kraka wellhead platform (WHP) is equally challenged by partly unknown state of SIR 
and the new questions raised about extreme waves in the North Sea that might overload the structure 
and lead to collapse.  
This work package proposes to initiate the needed improvement of state-of-the-art for SIR by using 
Kraka WHP as a case to confirm its adequate remaining life and to justify Advanced Water Flooding 
(AWF) investments. The approach taken will be to improve the knowledge base in two following main 
areas: 
 
The general knowledge about waves, the loading and structural response by supplying the Kraka 
platform with a monitoring system (pilot version). In addition to the generic value created, this will 
help validate the models used for SIR assessment. 
 
Specific knowledge about abnormal waves that might be a threat to the platform in case of storms. 
 
Solving these challenges will also  
 
Help inform the LEAN WHP project 
 
Provide better information for ultimate load safety 
 
Prioritization of Activities 
Based on the state-of-the-art review from the 19th International Ship and Offshore Structures 
Congress (ISSC 2015) in Cascais, Portugal, 7–10 September 2015 (Enclosure 1) and a technology 
==PAGE:220==
 
 
 
2
mapping exercise performed with assistance from industry and academia in the fall of 2015, the 
DHRTC has established the attached SIR Technology Map (Enclosure 2).  
A priori, the most significant source of uncertainty appears to be the characteristics of the 10,000 year 
extreme wave event in the offshore environment considering the complex interaction between waves, 
wind, current, seabed topography and the structures.  
Other predominant sources of uncertainty are the initial state of structural defects (Probability of 
Detection) and the quality of the as built structural model (validation challenge). Beyond these, the 
ability to formally prioritize between the many identified research topics requires a Value of Information 
(VoI) model, which can quantify the contribution of each activity in terms of reduced risk and/or 
uncertainty of the risk assessment. Calculation time is an important practical aspect in establishing a 
useful VoI model. 
Consequently, the DHRTC has decided to initiate the following first batch of activities to significantly 
advance state-of-the-art in extreme wave forecasting and structural model validation as well as to 
enable the subsequent prioritization of SIR research: 
A. Abnormal waves - direct load estimation. Loads from classical column or alternatives, 
correlated with in-situ waves from offshore LIDAR measurements. Projected related Kraka 
platform and AWF flagship. 
B. Abnormal waves - indirect load estimation. Loads from structural response, waves from laser. 
Project related to Kraka platform and AWF flagship.  
C. Actual fatigue stress. Compare Strain Gauge (SG) with Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) 
estimates and the feasibility of Structural Health Monitoring based on deflection derivative 
measurements.  
D. Load Environment Modeling and Forecasting. Environmental load models are established 
using Big Data techniques and physical and empirical evidence. Models are utilized for 
efficient means of load environment updating and real time decision making on shutdown and 
evacuation of offshore facilities.  
E. Accelerated Probabilistic Response Modeling and Analysis. Development of models and 
efficient probabilistic mechanics analysis techniques for reliability and risk analysis of offshore 
facilities subject to extreme environmental load events. 
F. Value of Information and Risk Based Inspection Planning. Development of Vale of Information 
models facilitating that the service life costs and risks of offshore facilities especially with 
respect to fatigue crack growth are managed optimally 
The job advertisement for the 6 PhDs are in Enclosure 3. 
Once these activities are underway, synergetic activities will be initiated in collaboration with DHRTC 
partners based on firm value propositions.  
 
 
==PAGE:221==
 
 
 
3
Enclosure 1: ISSC 2015 Recommendations  
 
The aim of the International Ship and Offshore Structures Congress (ISSC) is to facilitate the 
evaluation and dissemination of results from recent investigations to make recommendations for 
standard design procedures and criteria, to discuss research in progress and planned, to identify 
areas requiring future research and to encourage international collaboration in furthering these aims. 
A representative cross-section of the world’s leading Structural integrity and Reliability experts from 
academia, class societies and industry were present at the 19th International Ship and Offshore 
Structures Congress (ISSC 2015) in Cascais, Portugal, 7–10 September 2015. With respect to 
offshore structures, their main findings and recommendations were: 
Environment and Load 
 
Studies dedicated to including wave breaking, external forcing (wind and current) and 
modulational instability when modelling rogue waves.  
 
Detailed investigations of meteorological and oceanographic conditions in which extreme and 
rogue waves occur together with analyses of field wave time series (uncertainty due to sampling 
variability may be a problem here) are needed to reach a consensus about probability of 
occurrence of rogue waves.  
 
Inclusion of nonlinear and rogue waves in commonly used codes for analyses of wave-structure 
interaction is strongly recommended. Rogue waves affect not only local loads but also global 
loads. 
 
Focus needs to be given to properly accounting for directional effects in design, assuring 
consistency between omnidirectional and directional criteria, to seasonality, spatial and non-
stationary statistics, and current profile as well as modelling of wind sea and swell for design and 
operational purposes. Utilization of frequency-directional wave spectra, archived by met-offices 
today, in this work is strongly recommended. 
 
Studies contributing to a systematic quantification of uncertainties of metocean description should 
continue both in academia and industry. It is also recommended to agree on common definitions 
of uncertainties within academia and industry. 
 
The 2015 ISSC I.1 Committee recognizes the significance of the IPCC (2013) findings and the 
conclusions drawn by the Panel. There are still significant uncertainties associated with climate 
change projections.  
 
The wave-in-deck problem has been investigated experimentally and numerically, but with very 
limited success due its fast moving, multiphase and highly turbulent nature, is not very amenable 
to accurate numerical simulation, although 3D models help to better understand and simulate the 
mechanisms. Qualitative benchmark studies are required to validate and improve numerical 
approaches.  
 
Quasi-Static Strength Calculation  
 
Multi-physics modelling is challenge, validation studies are scarce and verification schemes are 
missing 
 
Ultimate Strength 
==PAGE:222==
 
 
 
4
 
Nonlinear progressive collapse is essential to understand true reliability, resilience and 
survivability. 
 
The ultimate limit state is now applied as a basis of structural design and strength assessment, 
and it is now a mandatory task for structural design of ships and offshore structures. The nonlinear 
finite element method (FEM) has become a dominant computational approach for complex 
structural engineering problems with the ability to consider both geometrical and material 
nonlinearities. However, taking into account modelling effort and computing time when using FEM, 
it seems that most of the collapse analyses neglect the time dependent mass and inertia effects 
and imply quasi-static structural responses while ultimate dynamic responses of hull structures 
due to the damage of collision and wave loads are still rarely studied. 
 
Fatigue and Fracture 
 
Effect of geometric imperfections coming from manufacturing tolerances. 
 
There is an urgent need for more component testing results to better validate models and an 
update of rules, standards & guidelines. 
 
Design Rules & Guidelines 
 
Challenge is as much convergent design optimization as enabling divergent (disruptive) design 
 
Account for uncertainty, often path from functional spec through reliability, risk and ending up with 
prescriptive guidelines. 
 
Serviceability limit states 
 
Safety limit states 
 
Offshore code calibration with a rational treatment of uncertainties (incl. Value of Information in 
risk analysis)

Enclosure 2: DHRTC SIR Technology Map 
 
On November 26th, 2015 DHRTC held a workshop on Structural Integrity and Reliability (SIR) with the 
objective to identify R&D activities that have the potential to significantly enhance the value of 
structural integrity and reliability activities in the context of life extension for offshore DUC facilities. 
The following organisations participated in the workshop: 
 
Rambøll Oil & Gas 
 
LIC Engineering 
 
Akselos 
 
DHI 
 
Force Technologies 
 
Mærsk Olie og Gas 
 
Aarhus Universitet 
 
Danmarks Tekniske Universitet (MAN, BYG, MEK/FD, Wind, Compute, Elektro) 
 
DHRTC 
 
The outcome of the workshop is the attached DHRTC SIR Technology Map. 
 
Colour code: 
 
White boxes: Main value chain 
 
Green boxes: Overarching objective 
 
Yellow boxes: Main challenges and improvement target 
 
Amber boxes: Research topics identified in DHRTC workshop on November 26th, 2015  
 
Blue boxes: Research topics not mentioned in workshop 
 
Green shadow: activities recommended as high priority in workshop 
 
Red shadow: activities qualified as low priority in workshop 
 
Please note that the Technology Readiness Level ranking of activities has not yet been quantified 
objectively.

Appendix B
Worksheets
B.1
System concepts
The dierent variations of concepts facilitated a discussion of the consequences of the design
choices. The focus were on data gathering. The process was done in a silent brainstorm on
a morphology chart, each generating a concept, based on the means and then presenting
the concept. Pros and cons were discussed which fueled ideas for the next two concepts.
Upon reviewing the sub-function, Installation, it became clear it had minimal impact on
the other sub-functions. It were therefore not included in the concept generation.
B.1.1 System concept 1: The AUVator
B.1.2 System concept 2: Quick 'n' Dirty
B.1.3 System concept 3: The Good Old
B.1.4 System concept 4: Drop and hoist
B.1.5 System concept 5: AUV dog
B.1.6 System concept 6: The Topside Drop
B.1.7 System concept 7: Basket dump

The concepts were based on these sub-functions
• Installation
 Diver(high dexterity, high precision, tested method / low depth, high safety
risk, time restrained)
 Rov (Deep diving, low safety risk, good precision / big crew, tether management,
longer dive)
• Transportation
 Helicopter
 Supply ship
 Inspection ship
 Permanent installed
• Deployment
 Topside crane
 Dedicated winch
 Helicopter
 Supply ship
 Inspection ship
 Permanent submerged
• Data collection
 Diver
 Rov
 AUV

B.3
Basic form factor of adapter and sensor connection
Description
The worksheet facilitated the discussion on the basic form factor on both the
adapter and sensor. The main function were a uniform way of connection and
although many more congurations were considered with there pros and cons the
circular were in favor at the end. By this way the sensor have no distinct direction to
be placed in with eases the operation of the autonomous robot. The design also took
into account the uid ow prole.
==PAGE:244==
228
Worksheets
B.4
Quantied structures of adapter-sensor connection
Description
Variation of relative arrangement of the adapter and sensor. With the two
component, limited options are available. The selection dependent on smallest contact
surface and that the sensor didn't touch the pipe. Reason here is that marine fouling
must not interrupt the interface between the two parts. Solution 1 and 2 where chosen
for further detailing.
==PAGE:245==
Appendix B: Worksheets
229
B.5
Form interface of adapter and sensor
Description
Form variations of the complimenting interface between the adapter and sensor.
The focus were a low prole, self centering and orientation and as simple a design as
possible. The selected design had a simple connection interface, the adapter had a
protective, but low angled prole for minimum current impact and the sensor allowed
stacking on each other. With xed interface dimensions on both adapter and sensor it
simplify the interface tool for the AUV.
==PAGE:246==
230
Worksheets
B.6
Adapter-sensor magnet interface
Description
This worksheet draws the engagements of the magnets between the dierent parts.
The systematics shows the AUV should induce a current or a signal through 1-3 in
order to actively disconnect 4-5 interface. When placing the sensor unit the AUV only
needs to disconnect its own electro-permanent magnet.
==PAGE:247==
Appendix B: Worksheets
231
B.7
Form variations of adapter-sensor interface
Description
The basic function of the adapter-sensor interface had two requirements. Ensure a
strong connection and be able to disconnect. The assessment relied on the number of
interactions in order to obtain the requirements. The selection also relied on the
underwater environment, load and complexity. The magnet were superior in
comparison with its simple function. The main concerns with using a magnet is to
ensure the attracting force is greater than the unit will be exposed of.
==PAGE:248==
232
Worksheets
B.8
Disengagement of magnet
Description
The choice of using a magnet puts the question how to disengage it. This can either
be done with force or by a electro controlled magnet. Just using force is a simple
method, but the load on the monitoring unit could damage it. The signal to the
electro permanent magnet can either be discharged from the AUV or from the sensor
unit itself, but inducing the power from the AUV is a more complex task than to
rather just signal the task to the sensor. Calculations have shown that the power
consumption is so low that even if the system fails to disengage several times, there
will still be enough power.
==PAGE:249==
Appendix B: Worksheets
233
B.9
Quantied structures of pipe and adapter connection
Description
Variation of relative arrangement of the strain sensor, magnet, glue container
compared to the pipe. At this stage the we expected to have at least two strain sensors
and the size where unknown at this point. The selection process looked at both one
and two magnet. The focus were on secure contact to surface and a clever position of
the glue container. The choice were the encapsulated strain sensor, for better
protection and a guarantee the strain sensor would be attached. The glue container
ended up not being part of the monitoring unit.
==PAGE:250==
234
Worksheets
B.10
Form function on adhesive engagement
Description
Form function on how to apply the adhesive. Two initial ideas were to either screw
or press the adhesive that were contained in the monitoring unit, but ideas of the
dierent placement of the glue occurred. The selected choice were the added solution,
since it simplied the monitoring units design. The mounting should only be done
once and therefore the focus were to use as less space in the unit as possible. The
precoated solution had potential, but it was very vulnerable to particle contamination.
==PAGE:251==
Appendix B: Worksheets
235
B.11
Form function on adapter and ROV engagement
Description
A few concepts on how to engage the adapter with the ROV. The focus were to
engage the adapter in dierent ways. Industry practice is with a handle, but the
solution we selected were the magnet. This choice correlates with the rest of the
interfaces of the unit.
==PAGE:252==

B.12
Mounting process
Description
The notes list the process of mounting the adapter to the wall. The sequence
process highlights the dierent stages before completion. The considerations covered
the number of stages the process should go through. The the complex mounting
process need all 4 stages, but in future design the complexity could be lowered by the
fewer stages.
==PAGE:253==
Appendix B: Worksheets
237
B.13
Quantied structures pipe-adapter interface
Description
The sketches were early ideas for how the adhesive were injected. In the rst two
solutions the adhesive were preloaded in the unit. The focus here were to apply
adhesive under the strain sensor rst before the rest of the unit. The injection were
driven by the ROV arm in the screw press. Drawing 2 had elements of a adjustment
magnet before it would be permanent mounted and a spring feature to press the strain
sensor down. Drawing 3 bases on injecting the adhesive from the ROV. When the glue
runs through the membrane it pushes the strain sensor up, allowing the glue to cover
the area. After releasing the pressure the membrane reconnect with its magnets.
==PAGE:254==
238
Worksheets
B.14
Matrix of sensor replacement options
Description
In this worksheets the discussion were about how the changing sequence of the
sensor should be. Two options were considered with the focus on time, AUV alignment
and number of operation. With a rack of sensors the AUV could be changing far more
sensors without going back and forth between its basket. This require a magazine
design on the AUV and this will complicate the design requirements of the AUV
system further. For simplicity the rack was not selected, but could be implemented in
the future and the sensor design allows them to connect to each other. Two operation
change was chosen, even though the AUV should do the same alignment twice per
monitoring unit. The size of the adapter should had complimented two sensors for it
to work which complicated the design. The choices here were focused on simplicity,
but it does not exclude that the ideas could be implemented in a future design.

C.2
Autonomous Inspection Vehicle (AIV)- Subsea 7 AIV
==PAGE:264==
seabed-to-surface
liFe-of-field
SERVICE OVERVIEW
Autonomous 
Inspection 
Vehicle (AIV)
Subsea 7, one of the world’s leading subsea 
engineering and construction companies servicing 
the oil and gas industry, in collaboration with 
SeeByte, a leading provider of smart software 
solutions for unmanned underwater vehicles, has 
developed an AIV, which has the unique potential to 
revolutionise Life-of-Field projects. 
Key elements include:
• This technology has the potential to revolutionise Life-of-Field 
(LOF) projects by providing operators with a cost-effective, low-
risk inspection system to aid field survey, integrity management 
and intervention activities
• The AIV has the unique ability to operate directly from a host 
facility such as an FPSO or Platform as well as from infield 
support vessels or mobile rigs  
• The AIV has no tether which enhances vehicle manoeuvrability 
and the capability to access confined spaces 
• The AIV carries an array of navigation tools and sensors that 
are powered by its own onboard battery power source, which 
allows for up to 24 hours of autonomous inspection and potential 
intervention.
==PAGE:265==
www.subsea7.com
© Subsea 7, 2012. Information correct at time of going to press.
Mission 
As offshore oil and gas exploration and production is evolving into deeper water, 
more remote locations and with an associated increase in complexity, operators are 
looking towards a more intelligent and autonomous maintenance environment.  Whilst 
the existing heavy work class ROV and Diver intervention tasks will remain within 
the current technology, the time has come for the next innovation in terms of subsea 
inspection vehicles and methods.
Subsea 7’s vision is to use our experience and expertise, combined with SeeByte’s 
advanced software capabilities, to bring to the market a hover-capable autonomous 
and intelligent inspection vehicle. This Autonomous Inspection Vehicle (AIV) is designed 
to be launched from a host platform, from vessels of convenience or other ROVSVs 
within the field, in single or multiple vehicle mode). 
Base Case Deployment Scenarios 
The initial AIV (Mark 1) is capable of undertaking General Visual Inspection (GVI) 
of subsea infrastructure, including structures and risers within offshore fields.  The 
initial systems will carry the latest 3D sonars and cameras.  There are a number of 
deployment scenarios envisaged:
• The multi-AIV or “Lobster Pot” scenario. A field inspection campaign utilising a 
low-cost vessel of convenience, deploying 3 or more AIVs to optimise mission 
coverage.All data will be recovered and sent ashore for processing, only requiring a 
minimal deployment and maintenance crew onboard
• A similar inspection campaign or smaller packages of work can also be undertaken  
by a single AIV. These would be deployed from a vessel already within a field and 
work simultaneously from an RSV which is undertaking a construction campaign 
with standard WRoVs.
• A more permanent deployment on a fixed platform or FPSO; ready to be deployed 
whenever a specific visual inspection or planned inspection campaign is required – 
all at the operator’s convenience without the need for a vessel mobilisation.
Technical specification 
• Depth Rating	
3,000m
• Dimensions 	
1700mm (L) 800mm (H) 1300mm (W)
• Inspection sensors	
3D forward looking sonar 
	
Colour inspection class video camera  
	
Profiling sonar & downward look camera
• Communications	
Acoustic, Wi-Fi, Satellite
• Navigation:	
	
	
General	
Dead reckoning (1% distance travelled) aiding Sensor  
	
	
data & seabed markers
	
	
Pipeline	
Tracking using profiling sonar to optimise video  
	
	
coverage	at 1.5-3m range.
	
	
Riser	
Tracking using 3D sonar to optimise video coverage  
	
	
at 1.5- 3m range.
	
	
Structure	
Tracking using sonar and video to optimise video  
	
	
coverage at 1.5-3m range
